"54"	"A Whisper"
"55"	"to the Reader."
"56"	"There is no character, howsoever good and fine, but it can be destroyed"
"57"	"by ridicule, howsoever poor and witless. Observe the ass, for instance:"
"58"	"his character is about perfect, he is the choicest spirit among all the"
"59"	"humbler animals, yet see what ridicule has brought him to. Instead of"
"60"	"feeling complimented when we are called an ass, we are left in "
"61"	"doubt.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"62"	"A person who is ignorant of legal matters is always liable to make"
"63"	"mistakes when he tries to photograph a court scene with his pen; and so"
"64"	"I was not willing to let the law chapters in this book go to press"
"65"	"without first subjecting them to rigid and exhausting revision and"
"66"	"correction by a trained barrister--if that is what they are called."
"67"	"These chapters are right, now, in every detail, for they were rewritten"
"68"	"under the immediate eye of William Hicks, who studied law part of a"
"69"	"while in southwest Missouri thirty-five years ago and then came over"
"70"	"here to Florence for his health and is still helping for exercise and"
"71"	"board in Macaroni Vermicelli's horse-feed shed which is up the back"
"72"	"alley as you turn around the corner out of the Piazza del Duomo just"
"73"	"beyond the house where that stone that Dante used to sit on six hundred"
"74"	"years ago is let into the wall when he let on to be watching them build"
"75"	"Giotto's campanile and yet always got tired looking as soon as Beatrice"
"76"	"passed along on her way to get a chunk of chestnut cake to defend"
"77"	"herself with in case of a Ghibelline outbreak before she got to school,"
"78"	"at the same old stand where they sell the same old cake to this day and"
"79"	"it is just as light and good as it was then, too, and this is not"
"80"	"flattery, far from it. He was a little rusty on his law, but he rubbed"
"81"	"up for this book, and those two or three legal chapters are right and"
"82"	"straight, now. He told me so himself."
"83"	"Given under my hand this second day of January, 1893, at the Villa"
"84"	"Viviani, village of Settignano, three miles back of Florence, on the"
"85"	"hills--the same certainly affording the most charming view to be found"
"86"	"on this planet, and with it the most dreamlike and enchanting sunsets to"
"87"	"be found in any planet or even in any solar system--and given, too, in"
"88"	"the swell room of the house, with the busts of Cerretani senators and"
"89"	"other grandees of this line looking approvingly down upon me as they"
"90"	"used to look down upon Dante, and mutely asking me to adopt them into my"
"91"	"family, which I do with pleasure, for my remotest ancestors are but"
"92"	"spring chickens compared with these robed and stately antiques, and it"
"93"	"will be a great and satisfying lift for me, that six hundred years will."
"94"	"Mark Twain."
"95"	"CHAPTER I."
"96"	"Pudd'nhead Wins His Name."
"97"	"Tell the truth or trump--but get the trick.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's"
"98"	"Calendar."
"99"	"The scene of this chronicle is the town of Dawson's Landing, on the"
"100"	"Missouri side of the Mississippi, half a day's journey, per steamboat,"
"101"	"below St. Louis."
"102"	"In 1830 it was a snug little collection of modest one- and two-story"
"103"	"frame dwellings whose whitewashed exteriors were almost concealed from"
"104"	"sight by climbing tangles of rose-vines, honeysuckles, and"
"105"	"morning-glories. Each of these pretty homes had a garden in front fenced"
"106"	"with white palings and opulently stocked with hollyhocks, marigolds,"
"107"	"touch-me-nots, prince's-feathers and other old-fashioned flowers; while"
"108"	"on the window-sills of the houses stood wooden boxes containing"
"109"	"moss-rose plants and terra-cotta pots in which grew a breed of geranium"
"110"	"whose spread of intensely red blossoms accented the prevailing pink tint"
"111"	"of the rose-clad house-front like an explosion of flame. When there was"
"112"	"room on the ledge outside of the pots and boxes for a cat, the cat was"
"113"	"there--in sunny weather--stretched at full length, asleep and blissful,"
"114"	"with her furry belly to the sun and a paw curved over her nose. Then"
"115"	"that house was complete, and its contentment and peace were made"
"116"	"manifest to the world by this symbol, whose testimony is infallible. A"
"117"	"home without a cat--and a well-fed, well-petted and properly revered"
"118"	"cat--may be a perfect home, perhaps, but how can it prove title?"
"119"	"All along the streets, on both sides, at the outer edge of the brick"
"120"	"sidewalks, stood locust-trees with trunks protected by wooden boxing,"
"121"	"and these furnished shade for summer and a sweet fragrance in spring"
"122"	"when the clusters of buds came forth. The main street, one block back"
"123"	"from the river, and running parallel with it, was the sole business"
"124"	"street. It was six blocks long, and in each block two or three brick"
"125"	"stores three stories high towered above interjected bunches of little"
"126"	"frame shops. Swinging signs creaked in the wind, the street's whole"
"127"	"length. The candy-striped pole which indicates nobility proud and"
"128"	"ancient along the palace-bordered canals of Venice, indicated merely the"
"129"	"humble barber shop along the main street of Dawson's Landing. On a chief"
"130"	"corner stood a lofty unpainted pole wreathed from top to bottom with tin"
"131"	"pots and pans and cups, the chief tinmonger's noisy notice to the world"
"132"	"(when the wind blew) that his shop was on hand for business at that"
"133"	"corner."
"134"	"The hamlet's front was washed by the clear waters of the great river;"
"135"	"its body stretched itself rearward up a gentle incline; its most"
"136"	"rearward border fringed itself out and scattered its houses about the"
"137"	"base-line of the hills; the hills rose high, inclosing the town in a"
"138"	"half-moon curve, clothed with forests from foot to summit."
"139"	"Steamboats passed up and down every hour or so. Those belonging to the"
"140"	"little Cairo line and the little Memphis line always stopped; the big"
"141"	"Orleans liners stopped for hails only, or to land passengers or freight;"
"142"	"and this was the case also with the great flotilla of \"transients.\""
"143"	"These latter came out of a dozen rivers--the Illinois, the Missouri, the"
"144"	"Upper Mississippi, the Ohio, the Monongahela, the Tennessee, the Red"
"145"	"River, the White River, and so on; and were bound every whither and"
"146"	"stocked with every imaginable comfort or necessity which the"
"147"	"Mississippi's communities could want, from the frosty Falls of St."
"148"	"Anthony down through nine climates to torrid New Orleans."
"149"	"Dawson's Landing was a slaveholding town, with a rich slave-worked grain"
"150"	"and pork country back of it. The town was sleepy and comfortable and"
"151"	"contented. It was fifty years old, and was growing slowly--very slowly,"
"152"	"in fact, but still it was growing."
"153"	"The chief citizen was York Leicester Driscoll, about forty years old,"
"154"	"judge of the county court. He was very proud of his old Virginian"
"155"	"ancestry, and in his hospitalities and his rather formal and stately"
"156"	"manners he kept up its traditions. He was fine and just and generous. To"
"157"	"be a gentleman--a gentleman without stain or blemish--was his only"
"158"	"religion, and to it he was always faithful. He was respected, esteemed"
"159"	"and beloved by all the community. He was well off, and was gradually"
"160"	"adding to his store. He and his wife were very nearly happy, but not"
"161"	"quite, for they had no children. The longing for the treasure of a child"
"162"	"had grown stronger and stronger as the years slipped away, but the"
"163"	"blessing never came--and was never to come."
"164"	"With this pair lived the Judge's widowed sister, Mrs. Rachel Pratt, and"
"165"	"she also was childless--childless, and sorrowful for that reason, and"
"166"	"not to be comforted. The women were good and commonplace people, and did"
"167"	"their duty and had their reward in clear consciences and the community's"
"168"	"approbation. They were Presbyterians, the Judge was a free-thinker."
"169"	"Pembroke Howard, lawyer and bachelor, aged about forty, was another old"
"170"	"Virginian grandee with proved descent from the First Families. He was a"
"171"	"fine, brave, majestic creature, a gentleman according to the nicest"
"172"	"requirements of the Virginia rule, a devoted Presbyterian, an authority"
"173"	"on the \"code,\" and a man always courteously ready to stand up before you"
"174"	"in the field if any act or word of his had seemed doubtful or suspicious"
"175"	"to you, and explain it with any weapon you might prefer from brad-awls"
"176"	"to artillery. He was very popular with the people, and was the Judge's"
"177"	"dearest friend."
"178"	"Then there was Colonel Cecil Burleigh Essex, another F. F. V. of"
"179"	"formidable caliber--however, with him we have no concern."
"180"	"Percy Northumberland Driscoll, brother to the Judge, and younger than he"
"181"	"by five years, was a married man, and had had children around his"
"182"	"hearthstone; but they were attacked in detail by measles, croup and"
"183"	"scarlet fever, and this had given the doctor a chance with his effective"
"184"	"antediluvian methods; so the cradles were empty. He was a prosperous"
"185"	"man, with a good head for speculations, and his fortune was growing. On"
"186"	"the 1st of February, 1830, two boy babes were born in his house: one to"
"187"	"him, the other to one of his slave girls, Roxana by name. Roxana was"
"188"	"twenty years old. She was up and around the same day, with her hands"
"189"	"full, for she was tending both babies."
"190"	"Mrs. Percy Driscoll died within the week. Roxy remained in charge of the"
"191"	"children. She had her own way, for Mr. Driscoll soon absorbed himself in"
"192"	"his speculations and left her to her own devices."
"193"	"In that same month of February, Dawson's Landing gained a new citizen."
"194"	"This was Mr. David Wilson, a young fellow of Scotch parentage. He had"
"195"	"wandered to this remote region from his birthplace in the interior of"
"196"	"the State of New York, to seek his fortune. He was twenty-five years"
"197"	"old, college-bred, and had finished a post-college course in an Eastern"
"198"	"law school a couple of years before."
"199"	"He was a homely, freckled, sandy-haired young fellow, with an"
"200"	"intelligent blue eye that had frankness and comradeship in it and a"
"201"	"covert twinkle of a pleasant sort. But for an unfortunate remark of his,"
"202"	"he would no doubt have entered at once upon a successful career at"
"203"	"Dawson's Landing. But he made his fatal remark the first day he spent in"
"204"	"the village, and it \"gaged\" him. He had just made the acquaintance of a"
"205"	"group of citizens when an invisible dog began to yelp and snarl and howl"
"206"	"and make himself very comprehensively disagreeable, whereupon young"
"207"	"Wilson said, much as one who is thinking aloud--"
"208"	"\"I wish I owned half of that dog.\""
"209"	"\"Why?\" somebody asked."
"210"	"\"Because I would kill my half.\""
"211"	"The group searched his face with curiosity, with anxiety even, but found"
"212"	"no light there, no expression that they could read. They fell away from"
"213"	"him as from something uncanny, and went into privacy to discuss him. One"
"214"	"said:"
"215"	"\"'Pears to be a fool.\""
"216"	"\"'Pears?\" said another. \"Is, I reckon you better say.\""
"217"	"\"Said he wished he owned half of the dog, the idiot,\" said a third."
"218"	"\"What did he reckon would become of the other half if he killed his"
"219"	"half? Do you reckon he thought it would live?\""
"220"	"\"Why, he must have thought it, unless he is the downrightest fool in the"
"221"	"world; because if he hadn't thought it, he would have wanted to own the"
"222"	"whole dog, knowing that if he killed his half and the other half died,"
"223"	"he would be responsible for that half just the same as if he had killed"
"224"	"that half instead of his own. Don't it look that way to you, gents?\""
"225"	"\"Yes, it does. If he owned one half of the general dog, it would be so;"
"226"	"if he owned one end of the dog and another person owned the other end,"
"227"	"it would be so, just the same; particularly in the first case, because"
"228"	"if you kill one half of a general dog, there ain't any man that can tell"
"229"	"whose half it was, but if he owned one end of the dog, maybe he could"
"230"	"kill his end of it and--\""
"231"	"\"No, he couldn't either; he couldn't and not be responsible if the other"
"232"	"end died, which it would. In my opinion that man ain't in his right"
"233"	"mind.\""
"234"	"\"In my opinion he hain't got any mind.\""
"235"	"No. 3 said: \"Well, he's a lummox, anyway.\""
"236"	"\"That's what he is,\" said No. 4, \"he's a labrick--just a Simon-pure"
"237"	"labrick, if ever there was one.\""
"238"	"\"Yes, sir, he's a dam fool, that's the way I put him up,\" said No. 5."
"239"	"\"Anybody can think different that wants to, but those are my"
"240"	"sentiments.\""
"241"	"\"I'm with you, gentlemen,\" said No. 6. \"Perfect jackass--yes, and it"
"242"	"ain't going too far to say he is a pudd'nhead. If he ain't a pudd'nhead,"
"243"	"I ain't no judge, that's all.\""
"244"	"Mr. Wilson stood elected. The incident was told all over the town, and"
"245"	"gravely discussed by everybody. Within a week he had lost his first"
"246"	"name; Pudd'nhead took its place. In time he came to be liked, and well"
"247"	"liked too; but by that time the nickname had got well stuck on, and it"
"248"	"stayed. That first day's verdict made him a fool, and he was not able to"
"249"	"get it set aside, or even modified. The nickname soon ceased to carry"
"250"	"any harsh or unfriendly feeling with it, but it held its place, and was"
"251"	"to continue to hold its place for twenty long years."
"252"	"CHAPTER II."
"253"	"Driscoll Spares His Slaves."
"254"	"Adam was but human--this explains it all. He did not want the apple for"
"255"	"the apple's sake, he wanted it only because it was forbidden. The"
"256"	"mistake was in not forbidding the serpent; then he would have eaten the"
"257"	"serpent.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"258"	"Pudd'nhead Wilson had a trifle of money when he arrived, and he bought a"
"259"	"small house on the extreme western verge of the town. Between it and"
"260"	"Judge Driscoll's house there was only a grassy yard, with a paling fence"
"261"	"dividing the properties in the middle. He hired a small office down in"
"262"	"the town and hung out a tin sign with these words on it:"
"263"	"DAVID WILSON."
"264"	"ATTORNEY AND COUNSELOR-AT-LAW."
"265"	"SURVEYING, CONVEYANCING, ETC."
"266"	"But his deadly remark had ruined his chance--at least in the law. No"
"267"	"clients came. He took down his sign, after a while, and put it up on his"
"268"	"own house with the law features knocked out of it. It offered his"
"269"	"services now in the humble capacities of land-surveyor and expert"
"270"	"accountant. Now and then he got a job of surveying to do, and now and"
"271"	"then a merchant got him to straighten out his books. With Scotch"
"272"	"patience and pluck he resolved to live down his reputation and work his"
"273"	"way into the legal field yet. Poor fellow, he could not foresee that it"
"274"	"was going to take him such a weary long time to do it."
"275"	"He had a rich abundance of idle time, but it never hung heavy on his"
"276"	"hands, for he interested himself in every new thing that was born into"
"277"	"the universe of ideas, and studied it and experimented upon it at his"
"278"	"house. One of his pet fads was palmistry. To another one he gave no"
"279"	"name, neither would he explain to anybody what its purpose was, but"
"280"	"merely said it was an amusement. In fact he had found that his fads"
"281"	"added to his reputation as a pudd'nhead; therefore he was growing chary"
"282"	"of being too communicative about them. The fad without a name was one"
"283"	"which dealt with people's finger-marks. He carried in his coat pocket a"
"284"	"shallow box with grooves in it, and in the grooves strips of glass five"
"285"	"inches long and three inches wide. Along the lower edge of each strip"
"286"	"was pasted a slip of white paper. He asked people to pass their hands"
"287"	"through their hair (thus collecting upon them a thin coating of the"
"288"	"natural oil) and then make a thumb-mark on a glass strip, following it"
"289"	"with the mark of the ball of each finger in succession. Under this row"
"290"	"of faint grease-prints he would write a record on the strip of white"
"291"	"paper--thus:"
"292"	"John Smith, right hand--"
"293"	"and add the day of the month and the year, then take Smith's left hand"
"294"	"on another glass strip, and add name and date and the words \"left hand.\""
"295"	"The strips were now returned to the grooved box, and took their place"
"296"	"among what Wilson called his \"records.\""
"297"	"He often studied his records, examining and poring over them with"
"298"	"absorbing interest until far into the night; but what he found there--if"
"299"	"he found anything--he revealed to no one. Sometimes he copied on paper"
"300"	"the involved and delicate pattern left by the ball of a finger, and then"
"301"	"vastly enlarged it with a pantograph so that he could examine its web of"
"302"	"curving lines with ease and convenience."
"303"	"One sweltering afternoon--it was the first day of July, 1830--he was at"
"304"	"work over a set of tangled account-books in his work-room, which looked"
"305"	"westward over a stretch of vacant lots, when a conversation outside"
"306"	"disturbed him. It was carried on in yells, which showed that the people"
"307"	"engaged in it were not close together:"
"308"	"\"Say, Roxy, how does yo' baby come on?\" This from the distant voice."
"309"	"\"Fust-rate; how does you come on, Jasper?\" This yell was from close by."
"310"	"\"Oh, I's middlin'; hain't got noth'n' to complain of. I's gwine to come"
"311"	"a-court'n' you bimeby, Roxy.\""
"312"	"\"You is, you black mud-cat! Yah--yah--yah! I got somep'n' better to do"
"313"	"den 'sociat'n' wid niggers as black as you is. Is ole Miss Cooper's"
"314"	"Nancy done give you de mitten?\" Roxy followed this sally with another"
"315"	"discharge of care-free laughter."
"316"	"\"You's jealous, Roxy, dat's what's de matter wid you, you"
"317"	"hussy--yah--yah--yah! Dat's de time I got you!\""
"318"	"\"Oh, yes, you got me, hain't you. 'Clah to goodness if dat conceit o'"
"319"	"yo'n strikes in, Jasper, it gwine to kill you sho'. If you b'longed to"
"320"	"me I'd sell you down de river 'fo' you git too fur gone. Fust time I"
"321"	"runs acrost yo' marster, I's gwine to tell him so.\""
"322"	"This idle and aimless jabber went on and on, both parties enjoying the"
"323"	"friendly duel and each well satisfied with his own share of the wit"
"324"	"exchanged--for wit they considered it."
"325"	"Wilson stepped to the window to observe the combatants; he could not"
"326"	"work while their chatter continued. Over in the vacant lots was Jasper,"
"327"	"young, coal-black and of magnificent build, sitting on a wheelbarrow in"
"328"	"the pelting sun--at work, supposably, whereas he was in fact only"
"329"	"preparing for it by taking an hour's rest before beginning. In front of"
"330"	"Wilson's porch stood Roxy, with a local hand-made baby-wagon, in which"
"331"	"sat her two charges--one at each end and facing each other. From Roxy's"
"332"	"manner of speech, a stranger would have expected her to be black, but"
"333"	"she was not. Only one sixteenth of her was black, and that sixteenth did"
"334"	"not show. She was of majestic form and stature, her attitudes were"
"335"	"imposing and statuesque, and her gestures and movements distinguished by"
"336"	"a noble and stately grace. Her complexion was very fair, with the rosy"
"337"	"glow of vigorous health in the cheeks, her face was full of character"
"338"	"and expression, her eyes were brown and liquid, and she had a heavy suit"
"339"	"of fine soft hair which was also brown, but the fact was not apparent"
"340"	"because her head was bound about with a checkered handkerchief and the"
"341"	"hair was concealed under it. Her face was shapely, intelligent and"
"342"	"comely--even beautiful. She had an easy, independent carriage--when she"
"343"	"was among her own caste--and a high and \"sassy\" way, withal; but of"
"344"	"course she was meek and humble enough where white people were."
"345"	"To all intents and purposes Roxy was as white as anybody, but the one"
"346"	"sixteenth of her which was black outvoted the other fifteen parts and"
"347"	"made her a negro. She was a slave, and salable as such. Her child was"
"348"	"thirty-one parts white, and he, too, was a slave, and by a fiction of"
"349"	"law and custom a negro. He had blue eyes and flaxen curls like his white"
"350"	"comrade, but even the father of the white child was able to tell the"
"351"	"children apart--little as he had commerce with them--by their clothes:"
"352"	"for the white babe wore ruffled soft muslin and a coral necklace, while"
"353"	"the other wore merely a coarse tow-linen shirt which barely reached to"
"354"	"its knees, and no jewelry."
"355"	"The white child's name was Thomas à Becket Driscoll, the other's name"
"356"	"was Valet de Chambre: no surname--slaves hadn't the privilege. Roxana"
"357"	"had heard that phrase somewhere, the fine sound of it had pleased her"
"358"	"ear, and as she had supposed it was a name, she loaded it on to her"
"359"	"darling. It soon got shorted to \"Chambers,\" of course."
"360"	"Wilson knew Roxy by sight, and when the duel of wit began to play out,"
"361"	"he stepped outside to gather in a record or two. Jasper went to work"
"362"	"energetically, at once, perceiving that his leisure was observed. Wilson"
"363"	"inspected the children and asked--"
"364"	"\"How old are they, Roxy?\""
"365"	"\"Bofe de same age, sir--five months. Bawn de fust o' Feb'uary.\""
"366"	"\"They're handsome little chaps. One's just as handsome as the other,"
"367"	"too.\""
"368"	"A delighted smile exposed the girl's white teeth, and she said:"
"369"	"\"Bless yo' soul, Misto Wilson, it's pow'ful nice o' you to say dat,"
"370"	"'ca'se one of 'em ain't on'y a nigger. Mighty prime little nigger, I"
"371"	"al'ays says, but dat's 'ca'se it's mine, o' course.\""
"372"	"\"How do you tell them apart, Roxy, when they haven't any clothes on?\""
"373"	"Roxy laughed a laugh proportioned to her size, and said:"
"374"	"\"Oh, I kin tell 'em 'part, Misto Wilson, but I bet Marse Percy couldn't,"
"375"	"not to save his life.\""
"376"	"Wilson chatted along for awhile, and presently got Roxy's finger-prints"
"377"	"for his collection--right hand and left--on a couple of his glass"
"378"	"strips; then labeled and dated them, and took the \"records\" of both"
"379"	"children, and labeled and dated them also."
"380"	"Two months later, on the 3d of September, he took this trio of"
"381"	"finger-marks again. He liked to have a \"series,\" two or three \"takings\""
"382"	"at intervals during the period of childhood, these to be followed by"
"383"	"others at intervals of several years."
"384"	"The next day--that is to say, on the 4th of September--something"
"385"	"occurred which profoundly impressed Roxana. Mr. Driscoll missed another"
"386"	"small sum of money--which is a way of saying that this was not a new"
"387"	"thing, but had happened before. In truth it had happened three times"
"388"	"before. Driscoll's patience was exhausted. He was a fairly humane man"
"389"	"toward slaves and other animals; he was an exceedingly humane man toward"
"390"	"the erring of his own race. Theft he could not abide, and plainly there"
"391"	"was a thief in his house. Necessarily the thief must be one of his"
"392"	"negroes. Sharp measures must be taken. He called his servants before"
"393"	"him. There were three of these, besides Roxy: a man, a woman, and a boy"
"394"	"twelve years old. They were not related. Mr. Driscoll said:"
"395"	"\"You have all been warned before. It has done no good. This time I will"
"396"	"teach you a lesson. I will sell the thief. Which of you is the guilty"
"397"	"one?\""
"398"	"They all shuddered at the threat, for here they had a good home, and a"
"399"	"new one was likely to be a change for the worse. The denial was general."
"400"	"None had stolen anything--not money, anyway--a little sugar, or cake, or"
"401"	"honey, or something like that, that \"Marse Percy wouldn't mind or miss,\""
"402"	"but not money--never a cent of money. They were eloquent in their"
"403"	"protestations, but Mr. Driscoll was not moved by them. He answered each"
"404"	"in turn with a stern \"Name the thief!\""
"405"	"The truth was, all were guilty but Roxana; she suspected that the others"
"406"	"were guilty, but she did not know them to be so. She was horrified to"
"407"	"think how near she had come to being guilty herself; she had been saved"
"408"	"in the nick of time by a revival in the colored Methodist Church, a"
"409"	"fortnight before, at which time and place she \"got religion.\" The very"
"410"	"next day after that gracious experience, while her change of style was"
"411"	"fresh upon her and she was vain of her purified condition, her master"
"412"	"left a couple dollars lying unprotected on his desk, and she happened"
"413"	"upon that temptation when she was polishing around with a dust-rag. She"
"414"	"looked at the money awhile with a steady rising resentment, then she"
"415"	"burst out with--"
"416"	"\"Dad blame dat revival, I wisht it had 'a' be'n put off till to-morrow!\""
"417"	"Then she covered the tempter with a book, and another member of the"
"418"	"kitchen cabinet got it. She made this sacrifice as a matter of religious"
"419"	"etiquette; as a thing necessary just now, but by no means to be wrested"
"420"	"into a precedent; no, a week or two would limber up her piety, then she"
"421"	"would be rational again, and the next two dollars that got left out in"
"422"	"the cold would find a comforter--and she could name the comforter."
"423"	"Was she bad? Was she worse than the general run of her race? No. They"
"424"	"had an unfair show in the battle of life, and they held it no sin to"
"425"	"take military advantage of the enemy--in a small way; in a small way,"
"426"	"but not in a large one. They would smouch provisions from the pantry"
"427"	"whenever they got a chance; or a brass thimble, or a cake of wax, or an"
"428"	"emery-bag, or a paper of needles, or a silver spoon, or a dollar bill,"
"429"	"or small articles of clothing, or any other property of light value; and"
"430"	"so far were they from considering such reprisals sinful, that they would"
"431"	"go to church and shout and pray the loudest and sincerest with their"
"432"	"plunder in their pockets. A farm smoke-house had to be kept heavily"
"433"	"padlocked, for even the colored deacon himself could not resist a ham"
"434"	"when Providence showed him in a dream, or otherwise, where such a thing"
"435"	"hung lonesome and longed for some one to love. But with a hundred"
"436"	"hanging before him the deacon would not take two--that is, on the same"
"437"	"night. On frosty nights the humane negro prowler would warm the end of a"
"438"	"plank and put it up under the cold claws of chickens roosting in a tree;"
"439"	"a drowsy hen would step on to the comfortable board, softly clucking her"
"440"	"gratitude, and the prowler would dump her into his bag, and later into"
"441"	"his stomach, perfectly sure that in taking this trifle from the man who"
"442"	"daily robbed him of an inestimable treasure--his liberty--he was not"
"443"	"committing any sin that God would remember against him in the Last Great"
"444"	"Day."
"445"	"\"Name the thief!\""
"446"	"For the fourth time Mr. Driscoll had said it, and always in the same"
"447"	"hard tone. And now he added these words of awful import:"
"448"	"\"I give you one minute\"--he took out his watch. \"If at the end of that"
"449"	"time you have not confessed, I will not only sell all four of you,"
"450"	"but--I will sell you down the river!\""
"451"	"It was equivalent to condemning them to hell! No Missouri negro doubted"
"452"	"this. Roxy reeled in her tracks and the color vanished out of her face;"
"453"	"the others dropped to their knees as if they had been shot; tears gushed"
"454"	"from their eyes, their supplicating hands went up, and three answers"
"455"	"came in the one instant:"
"456"	"\"I done it!\""
"457"	"\"I done it!\""
"458"	"\"I done it!--have mercy, marster--Lord have mercy on us po' niggers!\""
"459"	"\"Very good,\" said the master, putting up his watch, \"I will sell you"
"460"	"here though you don't deserve it. You ought to be sold down the river.\""
"461"	"The culprits flung themselves prone, in an ecstasy of gratitude, and"
"462"	"kissed his feet, declaring that they would never forget his goodness and"
"463"	"never cease to pray for him as long as they lived. They were sincere,"
"464"	"for like a god he had stretched forth his mighty hand and closed the"
"465"	"gates of hell against them. He knew, himself, that he had done a noble"
"466"	"and gracious thing, and was privately well pleased with his magnanimity;"
"467"	"and that night he set the incident down in his diary, so that his son"
"468"	"might read it in after years, and be thereby moved to deeds of"
"469"	"gentleness and humanity himself."
"470"	"CHAPTER III."
"471"	"Roxy Plays a Shrewd Trick."
"472"	"Whoever has lived long enough to find out what life is, knows how deep a"
"473"	"debt of gratitude we owe to Adam, the first great benefactor of our"
"474"	"race. He brought death into the world.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"475"	"Percy Driscoll slept well the night he saved his house-minions from"
"476"	"going down the river, but no wink of sleep visited Roxy's eyes. A"
"477"	"profound terror had taken possession of her. Her child could grow up and"
"478"	"be sold down the river! The thought crazed her with horror. If she dozed"
"479"	"and lost herself for a moment, the next moment she was on her feet"
"480"	"flying to her child's cradle to see if it was still there. Then she"
"481"	"would gather it to her heart and pour out her love upon it in a frenzy"
"482"	"of kisses, moaning, crying, and saying, \"Dey sha'n't, oh, dey"
"483"	"sha'n't!--yo' po' mammy will kill you fust!\""
"484"	"Once, when she was tucking it back in its cradle again, the other child"
"485"	"nestled in its sleep and attracted her attention. She went and stood"
"486"	"over it a long time communing with herself:"
"487"	"\"What has my po' baby done, dat he couldn't have yo' luck? He hain't"
"488"	"done noth'n'. God was good to you; why warn't he good to him? Dey can't"
"489"	"sell you down de river. I hates yo' pappy; he hain't got no heart--for"
"490"	"niggers he hain't, anyways. I hates him, en I could kill him!\" She"
"491"	"paused awhile, thinking; then she burst into wild sobbings again, and"
"492"	"turned away, saying, \"Oh, I got to kill my chile, dey ain't no yuther"
"493"	"way,--killin' him wouldn't save de chile fum goin' down de river. Oh, I"
"494"	"got to do it, yo' po' mammy's got to kill you to save you, honey\"--she"
"495"	"gathered her baby to her bosom, now, and began to smother it with"
"496"	"caresses--\"Mammy's got to kill you--how kin I do it! But yo' mammy ain't"
"497"	"gwine to desert you--no, no; dah, don't cry--she gwine wid you, she"
"498"	"gwine to kill herself too. Come along, honey, come along wid mammy; we"
"499"	"gwine to jump in de river, den de troubles o' dis worl' is all over--dey"
"500"	"don't sell po' niggers down the river over yonder.\""
"501"	"She started toward the door, crooning to the child and hushing it;"
"502"	"midway she stopped, suddenly. She had caught sight of her new Sunday"
"503"	"gown--a cheap curtain-calico thing, a conflagration of gaudy colors and"
"504"	"fantastic figures. She surveyed it wistfully, longingly."
"505"	"\"Hain't ever wore it yet,\" she said, \"en it's jist lovely.\" Then she"
"506"	"nodded her head in response to a pleasant idea, and added, \"No, I ain't"
"507"	"gwine to be fished out, wid everybody lookin' at me, in dis mis'able ole"
"508"	"linsey-woolsey.\""
"509"	"She put down the child and made the change. She looked in the glass and"
"510"	"was astonished at her beauty. She resolved to make her death-toilet"
"511"	"perfect. She took off her handkerchief-turban and dressed her glossy"
"512"	"wealth of hair \"like white folks\"; she added some odds and ends of"
"513"	"rather lurid ribbon and a spray of atrocious artificial flowers; finally"
"514"	"she threw over her shoulders a fluffy thing called a \"cloud\" in that"
"515"	"day, which was of a blazing red complexion. Then she was ready for the"
"516"	"tomb."
"517"	"She gathered up her baby once more; but when her eye fell upon its"
"518"	"miserably short little gray tow-linen shirt and noted the contrast"
"519"	"between its pauper shabbiness and her own volcanic irruption of infernal"
"520"	"splendors, her mother-heart was touched, and she was ashamed."
"521"	"\"No, dolling, mammy ain't gwine to treat you so. De angels is gwine to"
"522"	"'mire you jist as much as dey does yo' mammy. Ain't gwine to have 'em"
"523"	"putt'n' dey han's up 'fo' dey eyes en sayin' to David en Goliah en dem"
"524"	"yuther prophets, 'Dat chile is dress' too indelicate fo' dis place.'\""
"525"	"By this time she had stripped off the shirt. Now she clothed the naked"
"526"	"little creature in one of Thomas à Becket's snowy long baby-gowns, with"
"527"	"its bright blue bows and dainty flummery of ruffles."
"528"	"\"Dah--now you's fixed.\" She propped the child in a chair and stood off"
"529"	"to inspect it. Straightway her eyes began to widen with astonishment and"
"530"	"admiration, and she clapped her hands and cried out, \"Why, it do beat"
"531"	"all!--I never knowed you was so lovely. Marse Tommy ain't a bit"
"532"	"puttier--not a single bit.\""
"533"	"She stepped over and glanced at the other infant; she flung a glance"
"534"	"back at her own; then one more at the heir of the house. Now a strange"
"535"	"light dawned in her eyes, and in a moment she was lost in thought. She"
"536"	"seemed in a trance; when she came out of it she muttered, \"When I 'uz"
"537"	"a-washin' 'em in de tub, yistiddy, his own pappy asked me which of 'em"
"538"	"was his'n.\""
"539"	"She began to move about like one in a dream. She undressed Thomas à"
"540"	"Becket, stripping him of everything, and put the tow-linen shirt on him."
"541"	"She put his coral necklace on her own child's neck. Then she placed the"
"542"	"children side by side, and after earnest inspection she muttered--"
"543"	"\"Now who would b'lieve clo'es could do de like o' dat? Dog my cats if it"
"544"	"ain't all I kin do to tell t'other fum which, let alone his pappy.\""
"545"	"She put her cub in Tommy's elegant cradle and said--"
"546"	"\"You's young Marse Tom fum dis out, en I got to practise and git used to"
"547"	"'memberin' to call you dat, honey, or I's gwine to make a mistake some"
"548"	"time en git us bofe into trouble. Dah--now you lay still en don't fret"
"549"	"no mo', Marse Tom--oh, thank de good Lord in heaven, you's saved, you's"
"550"	"saved!--dey ain't no man kin ever sell mammy's po' little honey down de"
"551"	"river now!\""
"552"	"She put the heir of the house in her own child's unpainted pine cradle,"
"553"	"and said, contemplating its slumbering form uneasily--"
"554"	"\"I's sorry for you, honey; I's sorry, God knows I is,--but what kin I"
"555"	"do, what could I do? Yo' pappy would sell him to somebody, some time, en"
"556"	"den he'd go down de river, sho', en I couldn't, couldn't, couldn't stan'"
"557"	"it.\""
"558"	"She flung herself on her bed and began to think and toss, toss and"
"559"	"think. By and by she sat suddenly upright, for a comforting thought had"
"560"	"flown through her worried mind--"
"561"	"\"'Tain't no sin--white folks has done it! It ain't no sin, glory to"
"562"	"goodness it ain't no sin! Dey's done it--yes, en dey was de biggest"
"563"	"quality in de whole bilin', too--kings!\""
"564"	"She began to muse; she was trying to gather out of her memory the dim"
"565"	"particulars of some tale she had heard some time or other. At last she"
"566"	"said--"
"567"	"\"Now I's got it; now I 'member. It was dat ole nigger preacher dat tole"
"568"	"it, de time he come over here fum Illinois en preached in de nigger"
"569"	"church. He said dey ain't nobody kin save his own self--can't do it by"
"570"	"faith, can't do it by works, can't do it no way at all. Free grace is de"
"571"	"on'y way, en dat don't come fum nobody but jis' de Lord; en he kin give"
"572"	"it to anybody he please, saint or sinner--he don't kyer. He do jis' as"
"573"	"he's a mineter. He s'lect out anybody dat suit him, en put another one"
"574"	"in his place, and make de fust one happy forever en leave t'other one to"
"575"	"burn wid Satan. De preacher said it was jist like dey done in Englan'"
"576"	"one time, long time ago. De queen she lef' her baby layin' aroun' one"
"577"	"day, en went out callin'; en one o' de niggers roun'-'bout de place dat"
"578"	"was 'mos' white, she come in en see de chile layin' aroun', en tuck en"
"579"	"put her own chile's clo'es on de queen's chile, en put de queen's"
"580"	"chile's clo'es on her own chile, en den lef' her own chile layin' aroun'"
"581"	"en tuck en toted de queen's chile home to de nigger-quarter, en nobody"
"582"	"ever foun' it out, en her chile was de king bimeby, en sole de queen's"
"583"	"chile down de river one time when dey had to settle up de estate. Dah,"
"584"	"now--de preacher said it his own self, en it ain't no sin, 'ca'se white"
"585"	"folks done it. Dey done it--yes, dey done it; en not on'y jis' common"
"586"	"white folks nuther, but de biggest quality dey is in de whole bilin'."
"587"	"Oh, I's so glad I 'member 'bout dat!\""
"588"	"She got up light-hearted and happy, and went to the cradles and spent"
"589"	"what was left of the night \"practising.\" She would give her own child a"
"590"	"light pat and say humbly, \"Lay still, Marse Tom,\" then give the real Tom"
"591"	"a pat and say with severity, \"Lay still, Chambers!--does you want me to"
"592"	"take somep'n' to you?\""
"593"	"As she progressed with her practice, she was surprised to see how"
"594"	"steadily and surely the awe which had kept her tongue reverent and her"
"595"	"manner humble toward her young master was transferring itself to her"
"596"	"speech and manner toward the usurper, and how similarly handy she was"
"597"	"becoming in transferring her motherly curtness of speech and"
"598"	"peremptoriness of manner to the unlucky heir of the ancient house of"
"599"	"Driscoll."
"600"	"She took occasional rests from practising, and absorbed herself in"
"601"	"calculating her chances."
"602"	"\"Dey'll sell dese niggers to-day fo' stealin' de money, den dey'll buy"
"603"	"some mo' dat don't know de chillen--so dat's all right. When I takes de"
"604"	"chillen out to git de air, de minute I's roun' de corner I's gwine to"
"605"	"gaum dey mouths all roun' wid jam, den dey can't nobody notice dey's"
"606"	"changed. Yes, I gwineter do dat till I's safe, if it's a year."
"607"	"\"Dey ain't but one man dat I's afeard of, en dat's dat Pudd'nhead"
"608"	"Wilson. Dey calls him a pudd'nhead, en says he's a fool. My lan', dat"
"609"	"man ain't no mo' fool den I is! He's de smartes' man in dis town, less'n"
"610"	"it's Jedge Driscoll or maybe Pem Howard. Blame dat man, he worries me"
"611"	"wid dem ornery glasses o' hisn; I b'lieve he's a witch. But nemmine, I's"
"612"	"gwine to happen aroun' dah one o' dese days en let on dat I reckon he"
"613"	"wants to print de chillen's fingers ag'in; en if he don't notice dey's"
"614"	"changed, I bound dey ain't nobody gwine to notice it, en den I's safe,"
"615"	"sho'. But I reckon I'll tote along a hoss-shoe to keep off de"
"616"	"witch-work.\""
"617"	"The new negroes gave Roxy no trouble, of course. The master gave her"
"618"	"none, for one of his speculations was in jeopardy, and his mind was so"
"619"	"occupied that he hardly saw the children when he looked at them, and all"
"620"	"Roxy had to do was to get them both into a gale of laughter when he came"
"621"	"about; then their faces were mainly cavities exposing gums, and he was"
"622"	"gone again before the spasm passed and the little creatures resumed a"
"623"	"human aspect."
"624"	"Within a few days the fate of the speculation became so dubious that Mr."
"625"	"Percy went away with his brother the Judge, to see what could be done"
"626"	"with it. It was a land speculation as usual, and it had gotten"
"627"	"complicated with a lawsuit. The men were gone seven weeks. Before they"
"628"	"got back Roxy had paid her visit to Wilson, and was satisfied. Wilson"
"629"	"took the finger-prints, labeled them with the names and with the"
"630"	"date--October the first--put them carefully away and continued his chat"
"631"	"with Roxy, who seemed very anxious that he should admire the great"
"632"	"advance in flesh and beauty which the babies had made since he took"
"633"	"their finger-prints a month before. He complimented their improvement to"
"634"	"her contentment; and as they were without any disguise of jam or other"
"635"	"stain, she trembled all the while and was miserably frightened lest at"
"636"	"any moment he--"
"637"	"But he didn't. He discovered nothing; and she went home jubilant, and"
"638"	"dropped all concern about the matter permanently out of her mind."
"639"	"CHAPTER IV."
"640"	"The Ways of the Changelings."
"641"	"Adam and Eve had many advantages, but the principal one was, that they"
"642"	"escaped teething.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"643"	"There is this trouble about special providences--namely, there is so"
"644"	"often a doubt as to which party was intended to be the beneficiary. In"
"645"	"the case of the children, the bears and the prophet, the bears got more"
"646"	"real satisfaction out of the episode than the prophet did, because they"
"647"	"got the children.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"648"	"This history must henceforth accommodate itself to the change which"
"649"	"Roxana has consummated, and call the real heir \"Chambers\" and the"
"650"	"usurping little slave \"Thomas à Becket\"--shortening this latter name to"
"651"	"\"Tom,\" for daily use, as the people about him did."
"652"	"\"Tom\" was a bad baby, from the very beginning of his usurpation. He"
"653"	"would cry for nothing; he would burst into storms of devilish temper"
"654"	"without notice, and let go scream after scream and squall after squall,"
"655"	"then climax the thing with \"holding his breath\"--that frightful"
"656"	"specialty of the teething nursling, in the throes of which the creature"
"657"	"exhausts its lungs, then is convulsed with noiseless squirmings and"
"658"	"twistings and kickings in the effort to get its breath, while the lips"
"659"	"turn blue and the mouth stands wide and rigid, offering for inspection"
"660"	"one wee tooth set in the lower rim of a hoop of red gums; and when the"
"661"	"appalling stillness has endured until one is sure the lost breath will"
"662"	"never return, a nurse comes flying, and dashes water in the child's"
"663"	"face, and--presto! the lungs fill, and instantly discharge a shriek, or"
"664"	"a yell, or a howl which bursts the listening ear and surprises the owner"
"665"	"of it into saying words which would not go well with a halo if he had"
"666"	"one. The baby Tom would claw anybody who came within reach of his nails,"
"667"	"and pound anybody he could reach with his rattle. He would scream for"
"668"	"water until he got it, and then throw cup and all on the floor and"
"669"	"scream for more. He was indulged in all his caprices, howsoever"
"670"	"troublesome and exasperating they might be; he was allowed to eat"
"671"	"anything he wanted, particularly things that would give him the"
"672"	"stomach-ache."
"673"	"When he got to be old enough to begin to toddle about and say broken"
"674"	"words and get an idea of what his hands were for, he was a more"
"675"	"consummate pest than ever. Roxy got no rest while he was awake. He would"
"676"	"call for anything and everything he saw, simply saying \"Awnt it!\" (want"
"677"	"it), which was a command. When it was brought, he said in a frenzy, and"
"678"	"motioning it away with his hands, \"Don't awnt it! don't awnt it!\" and"
"679"	"the moment it was gone he set up frantic yells of \"Awnt it! awnt it!"
"680"	"awnt it!\" and Roxy had to give wings to her heels to get that thing back"
"681"	"to him again before he could get time to carry out his intention of"
"682"	"going into convulsions about it."
"683"	"What he preferred above all other things was the tongs. This was because"
"684"	"his \"father\" had forbidden him to have them lest he break windows and"
"685"	"furniture with them. The moment Roxy's back was turned he would toddle"
"686"	"to the presence of the tongs and say \"Like it!\" and cock his eye to one"
"687"	"side to see if Roxy was observing; then, \"Awnt it!\" and cock his eye"
"688"	"again; then, \"Hab it!\" with another furtive glance; and finally, \"Take"
"689"	"it!\"--and the prize was his. The next moment the heavy implement was"
"690"	"raised aloft; the next, there was a crash and a squall, and the cat was"
"691"	"off on three legs to meet an engagement; Roxy would arrive just as the"
"692"	"lamp or a window went to irremediable smash."
"693"	"Tom got all the petting, Chambers got none. Tom got all the delicacies,"
"694"	"Chambers got mush and milk, and clabber without sugar. In consequence"
"695"	"Tom was a sickly child and Chambers wasn't. Tom was \"fractious,\" as Roxy"
"696"	"called it, and overbearing; Chambers was meek and docile."
"697"	"With all her splendid common sense and practical every-day ability, Roxy"
"698"	"was a doting fool of a mother. She was this toward her child--and she"
"699"	"was also more than this: by the fiction created by herself, he was"
"700"	"become her master; the necessity of recognizing this relation outwardly"
"701"	"and of perfecting herself in the forms required to express the"
"702"	"recognition, had moved her to such diligence and faithfulness in"
"703"	"practicing these forms that this exercise soon concreted itself into"
"704"	"habit; it became automatic and unconscious; then a natural result"
"705"	"followed: deceptions intended solely for others gradually grew"
"706"	"practically into self-deceptions as well; the mock reverence became real"
"707"	"reverence, the mock obsequiousness real obsequiousness, the mock homage"
"708"	"real homage; the little counterfeit rift of separation between"
"709"	"imitation-slave and imitation-master widened and widened, and became an"
"710"	"abyss, and a very real one--and on one side of it stood Roxy, the dupe"
"711"	"of her own deceptions, and on the other stood her child, no longer a"
"712"	"usurper to her, but her accepted and recognized master. He was her"
"713"	"darling, her master, and her deity all in one, and in her worship of him"
"714"	"she forgot who she was and what he had been."
"715"	"In babyhood Tom cuffed and banged and scratched Chambers unrebuked, and"
"716"	"Chambers early learned that between meekly bearing it and resenting it,"
"717"	"the advantage all lay with the former policy. The few times that his"
"718"	"persecutions had moved him beyond control and made him fight back had"
"719"	"cost him very dear at headquarters; not at the hands of Roxy, for if she"
"720"	"ever went beyond scolding him sharply for \"forgitt'n' who his young"
"721"	"marster was,\" she at least never extended her punishment beyond a box on"
"722"	"the ear. No, Percy Driscoll was the person. He told Chambers that under"
"723"	"no provocation whatever was he privileged to lift his hand against his"
"724"	"little master. Chambers overstepped the line three times, and got three"
"725"	"such convincing canings from the man who was his father and didn't know"
"726"	"it, that he took Tom's cruelties in all humility after that, and made no"
"727"	"more experiments."
"728"	"Outside of the house the two boys were together all through their"
"729"	"boyhood. Chambers was strong beyond his years, and a good fighter;"
"730"	"strong because he was coarsely fed and hard worked about the house, and"
"731"	"a good fighter because Tom furnished him plenty of practice--on white"
"732"	"boys whom he hated and was afraid of. Chambers was his constant"
"733"	"body-guard, to and from school; he was present on the playground at"
"734"	"recess to protect his charge. He fought himself into such a formidable"
"735"	"reputation, by and by, that Tom could have changed clothes with him, and"
"736"	"\"ridden in peace,\" like Sir Kay in Launcelot's armor."
"737"	"He was good at games of skill, too. Tom staked him with marbles to play"
"738"	"\"keeps\" with, and then took all the winnings away from him. In the"
"739"	"winter season Chambers was on hand, in Tom's worn-out clothes, with"
"740"	"\"holy\" red mittens, and \"holy\" shoes, and pants \"holy\" at the knees and"
"741"	"seat, to drag a sled up the hill for Tom, warmly clad, to ride down on;"
"742"	"but he never got a ride himself. He built snow men and snow"
"743"	"fortifications under Tom's directions. He was Tom's patient target when"
"744"	"Tom wanted to do some snowballing, but the target couldn't fire back."
"745"	"Chambers carried Tom's skates to the river and strapped them on him,"
"746"	"then trotted around after him on the ice, so as to be on hand when"
"747"	"wanted; but he wasn't ever asked to try the skates himself."
"748"	"In summer the pet pastime of the boys of Dawson's Landing was to steal"
"749"	"apples, peaches, and melons from the farmers' fruit-wagons,--mainly on"
"750"	"account of the risk they ran of getting their heads laid open with the"
"751"	"butt of the farmer's whip. Tom was a distinguished adept at these"
"752"	"thefts--by proxy. Chambers did his stealing, and got the peach-stones,"
"753"	"apple-cores, and melon-rinds for his share."
"754"	"Tom always made Chambers go in swimming with him, and stay by him as a"
"755"	"protection. When Tom had had enough, he would slip out and tie knots in"
"756"	"Chambers's shirt, dip the knots in the water and make them hard to undo,"
"757"	"then dress himself and sit by and laugh while the naked shiverer tugged"
"758"	"at the stubborn knots with his teeth."
"759"	"Tom did his humble comrade these various ill turns partly out of native"
"760"	"viciousness, and partly because he hated him for his superiorities of"
"761"	"physique and pluck, and for his manifold cleverness. Tom couldn't dive,"
"762"	"for it gave him splitting headaches. Chambers could dive without"
"763"	"inconvenience, and was fond of doing it. He excited so much admiration,"
"764"	"one day, among a crowd of white boys, by throwing back somersaults from"
"765"	"the stern of a canoe, that it wearied Tom's spirit, and at last he"
"766"	"shoved the canoe underneath Chambers while he was in the air--so he came"
"767"	"down on his head in the canoe-bottom; and while he lay unconscious,"
"768"	"several of Tom's ancient adversaries saw that their long-desired"
"769"	"opportunity was come, and they gave the false heir such a drubbing that"
"770"	"with Chambers's best help he was hardly able to drag himself home"
"771"	"afterward."
"772"	"When the boys were fifteen and upward, Tom was \"showing off\" in the"
"773"	"river one day, when he was taken with a cramp, and shouted for help. It"
"774"	"was a common trick with the boys--particularly if a stranger was"
"775"	"present--to pretend a cramp and howl for help; then when the stranger"
"776"	"came tearing hand over hand to the rescue, the howler would go on"
"777"	"struggling and howling till he was close at hand, then replace the howl"
"778"	"with a sarcastic smile and swim blandly away, while the town boys"
"779"	"assailed the dupe with a volley of jeers and laughter. Tom had never"
"780"	"tried this joke as yet, but was supposed to be trying it now, so the"
"781"	"boys held warily back; but Chambers believed his master was in earnest,"
"782"	"therefore he swam out, and arrived in time, unfortunately, and saved his"
"783"	"life."
"784"	"This was the last feather. Tom had managed to endure everything else,"
"785"	"but to have to remain publicly and permanently under such an obligation"
"786"	"as this to a nigger, and to this nigger of all niggers--this was too"
"787"	"much. He heaped insults upon Chambers for \"pretending\" to think he was"
"788"	"in earnest in calling for help, and said that anybody but a block-headed"
"789"	"nigger would have known he was funning and left him alone."
"790"	"Tom's enemies were in strong force here, so they came out with their"
"791"	"opinions quite freely. They laughed at him, and called him coward, liar,"
"792"	"sneak, and other sorts of pet names, and told him they meant to call"
"793"	"Chambers by a new name after this, and make it common in the town--\"Tom"
"794"	"Driscoll's niggerpappy,\"--to signify that he had had a second birth into"
"795"	"this life, and that Chambers was the author of his new being. Tom grew"
"796"	"frantic under these taunts, and shouted--"
"797"	"\"Knock their heads off, Chambers! knock their heads off! What do you"
"798"	"stand there with your hands in your pockets for?\""
"799"	"Chambers expostulated, and said, \"But, Marse Tom, dey's too many of"
"800"	"'em--dey's--\""
"801"	"\"Do you hear me?\""
"802"	"\"Please, Marse Tom, don't make me! Dey's so many of 'em dat--\""
"803"	"Tom sprang at him and drove his pocket-knife into him two or three times"
"804"	"before the boys could snatch him away and give the wounded lad a chance"
"805"	"to escape. He was considerably hurt, but not seriously. If the blade had"
"806"	"been a little longer his career would have ended there."
"807"	"Tom had long ago taught Roxy \"her place.\" It had been many a day now"
"808"	"since she had ventured a caress or a fondling epithet in his quarter."
"809"	"Such things, from a \"nigger,\" were repulsive to him, and she had been"
"810"	"warned to keep her distance and remember who she was. She saw her"
"811"	"darling gradually cease from being her son, she saw that detail perish"
"812"	"utterly; all that was left was master--master, pure and simple, and it"
"813"	"was not a gentle mastership, either. She saw herself sink from the"
"814"	"sublime height of motherhood to the somber depths of unmodified slavery."
"815"	"The abyss of separation between her and her boy was complete. She was"
"816"	"merely his chattel, now, his convenience, his dog, his cringing and"
"817"	"helpless slave, the humble and unresisting victim of his capricious"
"818"	"temper and vicious nature."
"819"	"Sometimes she could not go to sleep, even when worn out with fatigue,"
"820"	"because her rage boiled so high over the day's experiences with her boy."
"821"	"She would mumble and mutter to herself--"
"822"	"\"He struck me, en I warn't no way to blame--struck me in de face, right"
"823"	"before folks. En he's al'ays callin' me nigger-wench, en hussy, en all"
"824"	"dem mean names, when I's doin' de very bes' I kin. Oh, Lord, I done so"
"825"	"much for him--I lift' him away up to what he is--en dis is what I git"
"826"	"for it.\""
"827"	"Sometimes when some outrage of peculiar offensiveness stung her to the"
"828"	"heart, she would plan schemes of vengeance and revel in the fancied"
"829"	"spectacle of his exposure to the world as an imposter and a slave; but"
"830"	"in the midst of these joys fear would strike her: she had made him too"
"831"	"strong; she could prove nothing, and--heavens, she might get sold down"
"832"	"the river for her pains! So her schemes always went for nothing, and she"
"833"	"laid them aside in impotent rage against the fates, and against herself"
"834"	"for playing the fool on that fatal September day in not providing"
"835"	"herself with a witness for use in the day when such a thing might be"
"836"	"needed for the appeasing of her vengeance-hungry heart."
"837"	"And yet the moment Tom happened to be good to her, and kind,--and this"
"838"	"occurred every now and then,--all her sore places were healed, and she"
"839"	"was happy; happy and proud, for this was her son, her nigger son,"
"840"	"lording it among the whites and securely avenging their crimes against"
"841"	"her race."
"842"	"There were two grand funerals in Dawson's Landing that fall--the fall of"
"843"	"1845. One was that of Colonel Cecil Burleigh Essex, the other that of"
"844"	"Percy Driscoll."
"845"	"On his death-bed Driscoll set Roxy free and delivered his idolized"
"846"	"ostensible son solemnly into the keeping of his brother, the Judge and"
"847"	"his wife. Those childless people were glad to get him. Childless people"
"848"	"are not difficult to please."
"849"	"Judge Driscoll had gone privately to his brother, a month before, and"
"850"	"bought Chambers. He had heard that Tom had been trying to get his father"
"851"	"to sell the boy down the river, and he wanted to prevent the"
"852"	"scandal--for public sentiment did not approve of that way of treating"
"853"	"family servants for light cause or for no cause."
"854"	"Percy Driscoll had worn himself out in trying to save his great"
"855"	"speculative landed estate, and had died without succeeding. He was"
"856"	"hardly in his grave before the boom collapsed and left his hitherto"
"857"	"envied young devil of an heir a pauper. But that was nothing; his uncle"
"858"	"told him he should be his heir and have all his fortune when he died; so"
"859"	"Tom was comforted."
"860"	"Roxy had no home, now; so she resolved to go around and say good-by to"
"861"	"her friends and then clear out and see the world--that is to say, she"
"862"	"would go chambermaiding on a steamboat, the darling ambition of her race"
"863"	"and sex."
"864"	"Her last call was on the black giant, Jasper. She found him chopping"
"865"	"Pudd'nhead Wilson's winter provision of wood."
"866"	"Wilson was chatting with him when Roxy arrived. He asked her how she"
"867"	"could bear to go off chambermaiding and leave her boys; and chaffingly"
"868"	"offered to copy off a series of their finger-prints, reaching up to"
"869"	"their twelfth year, for her to remember them by; but she sobered in a"
"870"	"moment, wondering if he suspected anything; then she said she believed"
"871"	"she didn't want them. Wilson said to himself, \"The drop of black blood"
"872"	"in her is superstitious; she thinks there's some devilry, some"
"873"	"witch-business about my glass mystery somewhere; she used to come here"
"874"	"with an old horseshoe in her hand; it could have been an accident, but I"
"875"	"doubt it.\""
"876"	"CHAPTER V."
"877"	"The Twins Thrill Dawson's Landing."
"878"	"Training is everything. The peach was once a bitter almond; cauliflower"
"879"	"is nothing but cabbage with a college education.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's"
"880"	"Calendar."
"881"	"Remark of Dr. Baldwin's, concerning upstarts: We don't care to eat"
"882"	"toadstools that think they are truffles.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"883"	"Mrs. York Driscoll enjoyed two years of bliss with that prize,"
"884"	"Tom--bliss that was troubled a little at times, it is true, but bliss"
"885"	"nevertheless; then she died, and her husband and his childless sister,"
"886"	"Mrs. Pratt, continued the bliss-business at the old stand. Tom was"
"887"	"petted and indulged and spoiled to his entire content--or nearly that."
"888"	"This went on till he was nineteen, then he was sent to Yale. He went"
"889"	"handsomely equipped with \"conditions,\" but otherwise he was not an"
"890"	"object of distinction there. He remained at Yale two years, and then"
"891"	"threw up the struggle. He came home with his manners a good deal"
"892"	"improved; he had lost his surliness and brusqueness, and was rather"
"893"	"pleasantly soft and smooth, now; he was furtively, and sometimes openly,"
"894"	"ironical of speech, and given to gently touching people on the raw, but"
"895"	"he did it with a good-natured semiconscious air that carried it off"
"896"	"safely, and kept him from getting into trouble. He was as indolent as"
"897"	"ever and showed no very strenuous desire to hunt up an occupation."
"898"	"People argued from this that he preferred to be supported by his uncle"
"899"	"until his uncle's shoes should become vacant. He brought back one or two"
"900"	"new habits with him, one of which he rather openly"
"901"	"practised--tippling--but concealed another which was gambling. It would"
"902"	"not do to gamble where his uncle could hear of it; he knew that quite"
"903"	"well."
"904"	"Tom's Eastern polish was not popular among the young people. They could"
"905"	"have endured it, perhaps, if Tom had stopped there; but he wore gloves,"
"906"	"and that they couldn't stand, and wouldn't; so he was mainly without"
"907"	"society. He brought home with him a suit of clothes of such exquisite"
"908"	"style and cut and fashion,--Eastern fashion, city fashion,--that it"
"909"	"filled everybody with anguish and was regarded as a peculiarly wanton"
"910"	"affront. He enjoyed the feeling which he was exciting, and paraded the"
"911"	"town serene and happy all day; but the young fellows set a tailor to"
"912"	"work that night, and when Tom started out on his parade next morning he"
"913"	"found the old deformed negro bell-ringer straddling along in his wake"
"914"	"tricked out in a flamboyant curtain-calico exaggeration of his finery,"
"915"	"and imitating his fancy Eastern graces as well as he could."
"916"	"Tom surrendered, and after that clothed himself in the local fashion."
"917"	"But the dull country town was tiresome to him, since his"
"918"	"acquaintanceship with livelier regions, and it grew daily more and more"
"919"	"so. He began to make little trips to St. Louis for refreshment. There he"
"920"	"found companionship to suit him, and pleasures to his taste, along with"
"921"	"more freedom, in some particulars, than he could have at home. So,"
"922"	"during the next two years his visits to the city grew in frequency and"
"923"	"his tarryings there grew steadily longer in duration."
"924"	"He was getting into deep waters. He was taking chances, privately, which"
"925"	"might get him into trouble some day--in fact, did."
"926"	"Judge Driscoll had retired from the bench and from all business"
"927"	"activities in 1850, and had now been comfortably idle three years. He"
"928"	"was president of the Free-thinkers' Society, and Pudd'nhead Wilson was"
"929"	"the other member. The society's weekly discussions were now the old"
"930"	"lawyer's main interest in life. Pudd'nhead was still toiling in"
"931"	"obscurity at the bottom of the ladder, under the blight of that unlucky"
"932"	"remark which he had let fall twenty-three years before about the dog."
"933"	"Judge Driscoll was his friend, and claimed that he had a mind above the"
"934"	"average, but that was regarded as one of the Judge's whims, and it"
"935"	"failed to modify the public opinion. Or rather, that was one of the"
"936"	"reasons why it failed, but there was another and better one. If the"
"937"	"Judge had stopped with bare assertion, it would have had a good deal of"
"938"	"effect; but he made the mistake of trying to prove his position. For"
"939"	"some years Wilson had been privately at work on a whimsical almanac, for"
"940"	"his amusement--a calendar, with a little dab of ostensible philosophy,"
"941"	"usually in ironical form, appended to each date; and the Judge thought"
"942"	"that these quips and fancies of Wilson's were neatly turned and cute; so"
"943"	"he carried a handful of them around, one day, and read them to some of"
"944"	"the chief citizens. But irony was not for those people; their mental"
"945"	"vision was not focussed for it. They read those playful trifles in the"
"946"	"solidest earnest, and decided without hesitancy that if there had ever"
"947"	"been any doubt that Dave Wilson was a pudd'nhead--which there"
"948"	"hadn't--this revelation removed that doubt for good and all. That is"
"949"	"just the way in this world; an enemy can partly ruin a man, but it takes"
"950"	"a good-natured injudicious friend to complete the thing and make it"
"951"	"perfect. After this the Judge felt tenderer than ever toward Wilson, and"
"952"	"surer than ever that his calendar had merit."
"953"	"Judge Driscoll could be a free-thinker and still hold his place in"
"954"	"society because he was the person of most consequence in the community,"
"955"	"and therefore could venture to go his own way and follow out his own"
"956"	"notions. The other member of his pet organization was allowed the like"
"957"	"liberty because he was a cipher in the estimation of the public, and"
"958"	"nobody attached any importance to what he thought or did. He was liked,"
"959"	"he was welcome enough all around, but he simply didn't count for"
"960"	"anything."
"961"	"The widow Cooper--affectionately called \"aunt Patsy\" by everybody--lived"
"962"	"in a snug and comely cottage with her daughter Rowena, who was nineteen,"
"963"	"romantic, amiable, and very pretty, but otherwise of no consequence."
"964"	"Rowena had a couple of young brothers--also of no consequence."
"965"	"The widow had a large spare room which she let to a lodger, with board,"
"966"	"when she could find one, but this room had been empty for a year now, to"
"967"	"her sorrow. Her income was only sufficient for the family support, and"
"968"	"she needed the lodging-money for trifling luxuries. But now, at last, on"
"969"	"a flaming June day, she found herself happy; her tedious wait was ended;"
"970"	"her year-worn advertisement had been answered; and not by a village"
"971"	"applicant, oh, no!--this letter was from away off yonder in the dim"
"972"	"great world to the North: it was from St. Louis. She sat on her porch"
"973"	"gazing out with unseeing eyes upon the shining reaches of the mighty"
"974"	"Mississippi, her thoughts steeped in her good fortune. Indeed, it was"
"975"	"specially good fortune, for she was to have two lodgers instead of one."
"976"	"She had read the letter to the family, and Rowena had danced away to see"
"977"	"to the cleaning and airing of the room by the slave woman Nancy, and the"
"978"	"boys had rushed abroad in the town to spread the great news, for it was"
"979"	"matter of public interest, and the public would wonder and not be"
"980"	"pleased if not informed. Presently Rowena returned, all ablush with"
"981"	"joyous excitement, and begged for a re-reading of the letter. It was"
"982"	"framed thus:"
"983"	"Honored Madam: My brother and I have seen your advertisement, by chance,"
"984"	"and beg leave to take the room you offer. We are twenty-four years of"
"985"	"age and twins. We are Italians by birth, but have lived long in the"
"986"	"various countries of Europe, and several years in the United States. Our"
"987"	"names are Luigi and Angelo Capello. You desire but one guest; but dear"
"988"	"Madam, if you will allow us to pay for two, we will not incommode you."
"989"	"We shall be down Thursday."
"990"	"\"Italians! How romantic! Just think, ma--there's never been one in this"
"991"	"town, and everybody will be dying to see them, and they're all ours!"
"992"	"Think of that!\""
"993"	"\"Yes, I reckon they'll make a grand stir.\""
"994"	"\"Oh, indeed they will. The whole town will be on its head!"
"995"	"Think--they've been in Europe and everywhere! There's never been a"
"996"	"traveler in this town before. Ma, I shouldn't wonder if they've seen"
"997"	"kings!\""
"998"	"\"Well, a body can't tell, but they'll make stir enough, without that.\""
"999"	"\"Yes, that's of course. Luigi--Angelo. They're lovely names; and so"
"1000"	"grand and foreign--not like Jones and Robinson and such. Thursday they"
"1001"	"are coming, and this is only Tuesday; it's a cruel long time to wait."
"1002"	"Here comes Judge Driscoll in at the gate. He's heard about it. I'll go"
"1003"	"and open the door.\""
"1004"	"The Judge was full of congratulations and curiosity. The letter was read"
"1005"	"and discussed. Soon Justice Robinson arrived with more congratulations,"
"1006"	"and there was a new reading and a new discussion. This was the"
"1007"	"beginning. Neighbor after neighbor, of both sexes, followed, and the"
"1008"	"procession drifted in and out all day and evening and all Wednesday and"
"1009"	"Thursday. The letter was read and re-read until it was nearly worn out;"
"1010"	"everybody admired its courtly and gracious tone, and smooth and"
"1011"	"practised style, everybody was sympathetic and excited, and the Coopers"
"1012"	"were steeped in happiness all the while."
"1013"	"The boats were very uncertain in low water, in these primitive times."
"1014"	"This time the Thursday boat had not arrived at ten at night--so the"
"1015"	"people had waited at the landing all day for nothing; they were driven"
"1016"	"to their homes by a heavy storm without having had a view of the"
"1017"	"illustrious foreigners."
"1018"	"Eleven o'clock came; and the Cooper house was the only one in the town"
"1019"	"that still had lights burning. The rain and thunder were booming yet,"
"1020"	"and the anxious family were still waiting, still hoping. At last there"
"1021"	"was a knock at the door and the family jumped to open it. Two negro men"
"1022"	"entered, each carrying a trunk, and proceeded up-stairs toward the"
"1023"	"guest-room. Then entered the twins--the handsomest, the best dressed,"
"1024"	"the most distinguished-looking pair of young fellows the West had ever"
"1025"	"seen. One was a little fairer than the other, but otherwise they were"
"1026"	"exact duplicates."
"1027"	"CHAPTER VI."
"1028"	"Swimming in Glory."
"1029"	"Let us endeavor so to live that when we come to die even the undertaker"
"1030"	"will be sorry.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"1031"	"Habit is habit, and not to be flung out of the window by any man, but"
"1032"	"coaxed down-stairs a step at a time.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"1033"	"At breakfast in the morning the twins' charm of manner and easy and"
"1034"	"polished bearing made speedy conquest of the family's good graces. All"
"1035"	"constraint and formality quickly disappeared, and the friendliest"
"1036"	"feeling succeeded. Aunt Patsy called them by their Christian names"
"1037"	"almost from the beginning. She was full of the keenest curiosity about"
"1038"	"them, and showed it; they responded by talking about themselves, which"
"1039"	"pleased her greatly. It presently appeared that in their early youth"
"1040"	"they had known poverty and hardship. As the talk wandered along the old"
"1041"	"lady watched for the right place to drop in a question or two concerning"
"1042"	"that matter, and when she found it she said to the blond twin who was"
"1043"	"now doing the biographies in his turn while the brunette one rested--"
"1044"	"\"If it ain't asking what I ought not to ask, Mr. Angelo, how did you"
"1045"	"come to be so friendless and in such trouble when you were little? Do"
"1046"	"you mind telling? But don't if you do.\""
"1047"	"\"Oh, we don't mind it at all, madam; in our case it was merely"
"1048"	"misfortune, and nobody's fault. Our parents were well to do, there in"
"1049"	"Italy, and we were their only child. We were of the old Florentine"
"1050"	"nobility\"--Rowena's heart gave a great bound, her nostrils expanded, and"
"1051"	"a fine light played in her eyes--\"and when the war broke out my father"
"1052"	"was on the losing side and had to fly for his life. His estates were"
"1053"	"confiscated, his personal property seized, and there we were, in"
"1054"	"Germany, strangers, friendless, and in fact paupers. My brother and I"
"1055"	"were ten years old, and well educated for that age, very studious, very"
"1056"	"fond of our books, and well grounded in the German, French, Spanish, and"
"1057"	"English languages. Also, we were marvelous musical prodigies--if you"
"1058"	"will allow me to say it, it being only the truth."
"1059"	"\"Our father survived his misfortunes only a month, our mother soon"
"1060"	"followed him, and we were alone in the world. Our parents could have"
"1061"	"made themselves comfortable by exhibiting us as a show, and they had"
"1062"	"many and large offers; but the thought revolted their pride, and they"
"1063"	"said they would starve and die first. But what they wouldn't consent to"
"1064"	"do we had to do without the formality of consent. We were seized for the"
"1065"	"debts occasioned by their illness and their funerals, and placed among"
"1066"	"the attractions of a cheap museum in Berlin to earn the liquidation"
"1067"	"money. It took us two years to get out of that slavery. We traveled all"
"1068"	"about Germany receiving no wages, and not even our keep. We had to be"
"1069"	"exhibited for nothing, and beg our bread."
"1070"	"\"Well, madam, the rest is not of much consequence. When we escaped from"
"1071"	"that slavery at twelve years of age, we were in some respects men."
"1072"	"Experience had taught us some valuable things; among others, how to take"
"1073"	"care of ourselves, how to avoid and defeat sharks and sharpers, and how"
"1074"	"to conduct our own business for our own profit and without other"
"1075"	"people's help. We traveled everywhere--years and years--picking up"
"1076"	"smatterings of strange tongues, familiarizing ourselves with strange"
"1077"	"sights and strange customs, accumulating an education of a wide and"
"1078"	"varied and curious sort. It was a pleasant life. We went to Venice--to"
"1079"	"London, Paris, Russia, India, China, Japan--\""
"1080"	"At this point Nancy the slave woman thrust her head in at the door and"
"1081"	"exclaimed:"
"1082"	"\"Ole Missus, de house is plum' jam full o' people, en dey's jes"
"1083"	"a-spi'lin' to see de gen'lmen!\" She indicated the twins with a nod of"
"1084"	"her head, and tucked it back out of sight again."
"1085"	"It was a proud occasion for the widow, and she promised herself high"
"1086"	"satisfaction in showing off her fine foreign birds before her neighbors"
"1087"	"and friends--simple folk who had hardly ever seen a foreigner of any"
"1088"	"kind, and never one of any distinction or style. Yet her feeling was"
"1089"	"moderate indeed when contrasted with Rowena's. Rowena was in the clouds,"
"1090"	"she walked on air; this was to be the greatest day, the most romantic"
"1091"	"episode, in the colorless history of that dull country town. She was to"
"1092"	"be familiarly near the source of its glory and feel the full flood of it"
"1093"	"pour over her and about her; the other girls could only gaze and envy,"
"1094"	"not partake."
"1095"	"The widow was ready, Rowena was ready, so also were the foreigners."
"1096"	"The party moved along the hall, the twins in advance, and entered the"
"1097"	"open parlor door, whence issued a low hum of conversation. The twins"
"1098"	"took a position near the door, the widow stood at Luigi's side, Rowena"
"1099"	"stood beside Angelo, and the march-past and the introductions began. The"
"1100"	"widow was all smiles and contentment. She received the procession and"
"1101"	"passed it on to Rowena."
"1102"	"\"Good mornin', Sister Cooper\"--hand-shake."
"1103"	"\"Good morning, Brother Higgins--Count Luigi Capello, Mr. "
"1104"	"Higgins\"--hand-shake, followed by a devouring stare and \"I'm glad to see"
"1105"	"ye,\" on the part of Higgins, and a courteous inclination of the head and"
"1106"	"a pleasant \"Most happy!\" on the part of Count Luigi."
"1107"	"\"Good mornin', Roweny\"--hand-shake."
"1108"	"\"Good morning, Mr. Higgins--present you to Count Angelo Capello.\""
"1109"	"Hand-shake, admiring stare, \"Glad to see ye,\"--courteous nod, smily"
"1110"	"\"Most happy!\" and Higgins passes on."
"1111"	"None of these visitors was at ease, but, being honest people, they"
"1112"	"didn't pretend to be. None of them had ever seen a person bearing a"
"1113"	"title of nobility before, and none had been expecting to see one now,"
"1114"	"consequently the title came upon them as a kind of pile-driving surprise"
"1115"	"and caught them unprepared. A few tried to rise to the emergency, and"
"1116"	"got out an awkward \"My lord,\" or \"Your lordship,\" or something of that"
"1117"	"sort, but the great majority were overwhelmed by the unaccustomed word"
"1118"	"and its dim and awful associations with gilded courts and stately"
"1119"	"ceremony and anointed kingship, so they only fumbled through the"
"1120"	"hand-shake and passed on, speechless. Now and then, as happens at all"
"1121"	"receptions everywhere, a more than ordinary friendly soul blocked the"
"1122"	"procession and kept it waiting while he inquired how the brothers liked"
"1123"	"the village, and how long they were going to stay, and if their families"
"1124"	"were well, and dragged in the weather, and hoped it would get cooler"
"1125"	"soon, and all that sort of thing, so as to be able to say, when they got"
"1126"	"home, \"I had quite a long talk with them\"; but nobody did or said"
"1127"	"anything of a regrettable kind, and so the great affair went through to"
"1128"	"the end in a creditable and satisfactory fashion."
"1129"	"General conversation followed, and the twins drifted about from group to"
"1130"	"group, talking easily and fluently and winning approval, compelling"
"1131"	"admiration and achieving favor from all. The widow followed their"
"1132"	"conquering march with a proud eye, and every now and then Rowena said to"
"1133"	"herself with deep satisfaction, \"And to think they are ours--all ours!\""
"1134"	"There were no idle moments for mother or daughter. Eager inquiries"
"1135"	"concerning the twins were pouring into their enchanted ears all the"
"1136"	"time; each was the constant center of a group of breathless listeners;"
"1137"	"each recognized that she knew now for the first time the real meaning of"
"1138"	"that great word Glory, and perceived the stupendous value of it, and"
"1139"	"understood why men in all ages had been willing to throw away meaner"
"1140"	"happinesses, treasure, life itself, to get a taste of its sublime and"
"1141"	"supreme joy. Napoleon and all his kind stood accounted for--and"
"1142"	"justified."
"1143"	"When Rowena had at last done all her duty by the people in the parlor,"
"1144"	"she went up-stairs to satisfy the longings of an overflow-meeting there,"
"1145"	"for the parlor was not big enough to hold all the comers. Again she was"
"1146"	"besieged by eager questioners and again she swam in sunset seas of"
"1147"	"glory. When the forenoon was nearly gone, she recognized with a pang"
"1148"	"that this most splendid episode of her life was almost over, that"
"1149"	"nothing could prolong it, that nothing quite its equal could ever fall"
"1150"	"to her fortune again. But never mind, it was sufficient unto itself, the"
"1151"	"grand occasion had moved on an ascending scale from the start, and was a"
"1152"	"noble and memorable success. If the twins could but do some crowning"
"1153"	"act, now, to climax it, something unusual, something startling,"
"1154"	"something to concentrate upon themselves the company's loftiest"
"1155"	"admiration, something in the nature of an electric surprise--"
"1156"	"Here a prodigious slam-banging broke out below, and everybody rushed"
"1157"	"down to see. It was the twins knocking out a classic four-handed piece"
"1158"	"on the piano, in great style. Rowena was satisfied--satisfied down to"
"1159"	"the bottom of her heart."
