"1160"	"The young strangers were kept long at the piano. The villagers were"
"1161"	"astonished and enchanted with the magnificence of their performance, and"
"1162"	"could not bear to have them stop. All the music that they had ever heard"
"1163"	"before seemed spiritless prentice-work and barren of grace or charm when"
"1164"	"compared with these intoxicating floods of melodious sound. They"
"1165"	"realized that for once in their lives they were hearing masters."
"1166"	"CHAPTER VII."
"1167"	"The Unknown Nymph"
"1168"	"One of the most striking differences between a cat and a lie is that a"
"1169"	"cat has only nine lives.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"1170"	"The company broke up reluctantly, and drifted toward their several"
"1171"	"homes, chatting with vivacity, and all agreeing that it would be many a"
"1172"	"long day before Dawson's Landing would see the equal of this one again."
"1173"	"The twins had accepted several invitations while the reception was in"
"1174"	"progress, and had also volunteered to play some duets at an amateur"
"1175"	"entertainment for the benefit of a local charity. Society was eager to"
"1176"	"receive them to its bosom. Judge Driscoll had the good fortune to secure"
"1177"	"them for an immediate drive, and to be the first to display them in"
"1178"	"public. They entered his buggy with him, and were paraded down the main"
"1179"	"street, everybody flocking to the windows and sidewalks to see."
"1180"	"The Judge showed the strangers the new graveyard, and the jail, and"
"1181"	"where the richest man lived, and the Freemasons' hall, and the Methodist"
"1182"	"church, and the Presbyterian church, and where the Baptist church was"
"1183"	"going to be when they got some money to build it with, and showed them"
"1184"	"the town hall and the slaughter-house, and got out the independent fire"
"1185"	"company in uniform and had them put out an imaginary fire; then he let"
"1186"	"them inspect the muskets of the militia company, and poured out an"
"1187"	"exhaustless stream of enthusiasm over all these splendors, and seemed"
"1188"	"very well satisfied with the responses he got, for the twins admired his"
"1189"	"admiration, and paid him back the best they could, though they could"
"1190"	"have done better if some fifteen or sixteen hundred thousand previous"
"1191"	"experiences of this sort in various countries had not already rubbed off"
"1192"	"a considerable part of the novelty of it."
"1193"	"The Judge laid himself out hospitably to make them have a good time, and"
"1194"	"if there was a defect anywhere it was not his fault. He told them a good"
"1195"	"many humorous anecdotes, and always forgot the nub, but they were always"
"1196"	"able to furnish it, for these yarns were of a pretty early vintage, and"
"1197"	"they had had many a rejuvenating pull at them before. And he told them"
"1198"	"all about his several dignities, and how he had held this and that and"
"1199"	"the other place of honor or profit, and had once been to the"
"1200"	"legislature, and was now president of the Society of Free-thinkers. He"
"1201"	"said the society had been in existence four years, and already had two"
"1202"	"members, and was firmly established. He would call for the brothers in"
"1203"	"the evening if they would like to attend a meeting of it."
"1204"	"Accordingly he called for them, and on the way he told them all about"
"1205"	"Pudd'nhead Wilson, in order that they might get a favorable impression"
"1206"	"of him in advance and be prepared to like him. This scheme"
"1207"	"succeeded--the favorable impression was achieved. Later it was confirmed"
"1208"	"and solidified when Wilson proposed that out of courtesy to the"
"1209"	"strangers the usual topics be put aside and the hour be devoted to"
"1210"	"conversation upon ordinary subjects and the cultivation of friendly"
"1211"	"relations and good-fellowship,--a proposition which was put to vote and"
"1212"	"carried."
"1213"	"The hour passed quickly away in lively talk, and when it was ended the"
"1214"	"lonesome and neglected Wilson was richer by two friends than he had been"
"1215"	"when it began. He invited the twins to look in at his lodgings,"
"1216"	"presently, after disposing of an intervening engagement, and they"
"1217"	"accepted with pleasure."
"1218"	"Toward the middle of the evening they found themselves on the road to"
"1219"	"his house. Pudd'nhead was at home waiting for them and putting in his"
"1220"	"time puzzling over a thing which had come under his notice that morning."
"1221"	"The matter was this: He happened to be up very early--at dawn, in fact;"
"1222"	"and he crossed the hall which divided his cottage through the center,"
"1223"	"and entered a room to get something there. The window of the room had no"
"1224"	"curtains, for that side of the house had long been unoccupied, and"
"1225"	"through this window he caught sight of something which surprised and"
"1226"	"interested him. It was a young woman--a young woman where properly no"
"1227"	"young woman belonged; for she was in Judge Driscoll's house, and in the"
"1228"	"bedroom over the Judge's private study or sitting-room. This was young"
"1229"	"Tom Driscoll's bedroom. He and the Judge, the Judge's widowed sister"
"1230"	"Mrs. Pratt and three negro servants were the only people who belonged in"
"1231"	"the house. Who, then, might this young lady be? The two houses were"
"1232"	"separated by an ordinary yard, with a low fence running back through its"
"1233"	"middle from the street in front to the lane in the rear. The distance"
"1234"	"was not great, and Wilson was able to see the girl very well, the"
"1235"	"window-shades of the room she was in being up, and the window also. The"
"1236"	"girl had on a neat and trim summer dress, patterned in broad stripes of"
"1237"	"pink and white, and her bonnet was equipped with a pink veil. She was"
"1238"	"practising steps, gaits and attitudes, apparently; she was doing the"
"1239"	"thing gracefully, and was very much absorbed in her work. Who could she"
"1240"	"be, and how came she to be in young Tom Driscoll's room?"
"1241"	"Wilson had quickly chosen a position from which he could watch the girl"
"1242"	"without running much risk of being seen by her, and he remained there"
"1243"	"hoping she would raise her veil and betray her face. But she"
"1244"	"disappointed him. After a matter of twenty minutes she disappeared, and"
"1245"	"although he stayed at his post half an hour longer, she came no more."
"1246"	"Toward noon he dropped in at the Judge's and talked with Mrs. Pratt"
"1247"	"about the great event of the day, the levee of the distinguished"
"1248"	"foreigners at Aunt Patsy Cooper's. He asked after her nephew Tom, and"
"1249"	"she said he was on his way home, and that she was expecting him to"
"1250"	"arrive a little before night; and added that she and the Judge were"
"1251"	"gratified to gather from his letters that he was conducting himself very"
"1252"	"nicely and creditably--at which Wilson winked to himself privately."
"1253"	"Wilson did not ask if there was a newcomer in the house, but he asked"
"1254"	"questions that would have brought light-throwing answers as to that"
"1255"	"matter if Mrs. Pratt had had any light to throw; so he went away"
"1256"	"satisfied that he knew of things that were going on in her house of"
"1257"	"which she herself was not aware."
"1258"	"He was now waiting for the twins, and still puzzling over the problem of"
"1259"	"who that girl might be, and how she happened to be in that young"
"1260"	"fellow's room at daybreak in the morning."
"1261"	"CHAPTER VIII."
"1262"	"Marse Tom Tramples His Chance."
"1263"	"The holy passion of Friendship is of so sweet and steady and loyal and"
"1264"	"enduring a nature that it will last through a whole lifetime, if not"
"1265"	"asked to lend money.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"1266"	"Consider well the proportions of things. It is better to be a young"
"1267"	"June-bug than an old bird of paradise.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"1268"	"It is necessary now, to hunt up Roxy."
"1269"	"At the time she was set free and went away chambermaiding, she was"
"1270"	"thirty-five. She got a berth as second chambermaid on a Cincinnati boat"
"1271"	"in the New Orleans trade, the Grand Mogul. A couple of trips made her"
"1272"	"wonted and easy-going at the work, and infatuated her with the stir and"
"1273"	"adventure and independence of steamboat life. Then she was promoted and"
"1274"	"became head chambermaid. She was a favorite with the officers, and"
"1275"	"exceedingly proud of their joking and friendly way with her."
"1276"	"During eight years she served three parts of the year on that boat, and"
"1277"	"the winters on a Vicksburg packet. But now for two months she had had"
"1278"	"rheumatism in her arms, and was obliged to let the wash-tub alone. So"
"1279"	"she resigned. But she was well fixed--rich, as she would have described"
"1280"	"it; for she had lived a steady life, and had banked four dollars every"
"1281"	"month in New Orleans as a provision for her old age. She said in the"
"1282"	"start that she had \"put shoes on one bar'footed nigger to tromple on her"
"1283"	"with,\" and that one mistake like that was enough; she would be"
"1284"	"independent of the human race thenceforth forevermore if hard work and"
"1285"	"economy could accomplish it. When the boat touched the levee at New"
"1286"	"Orleans she bade good-by to her comrades on the Grand Mogul and moved"
"1287"	"her kit ashore."
"1288"	"But she was back in a hour. The bank had gone to smash and carried her"
"1289"	"four hundred dollars with it. She was a pauper, and homeless. Also"
"1290"	"disabled bodily, at least for the present. The officers were full of"
"1291"	"sympathy for her in her trouble, and made up a little purse for her. She"
"1292"	"resolved to go to her birthplace; she had friends there among the"
"1293"	"negroes, and the unfortunate always help the unfortunate, she was well"
"1294"	"aware of that; those lowly comrades of her youth would not let her"
"1295"	"starve."
"1296"	"She took the little local packet at Cairo, and now she was on the"
"1297"	"home-stretch. Time had worn away her bitterness against her son, and she"
"1298"	"was able to think of him with serenity. She put the vile side of him out"
"1299"	"of her mind, and dwelt only on recollections of his occasional acts of"
"1300"	"kindness to her. She gilded and otherwise decorated these, and made them"
"1301"	"very pleasant to contemplate. She began to long to see him. She would go"
"1302"	"and fawn upon him, slave-like--for this would have to be her attitude,"
"1303"	"of course--and maybe she would find that time had modified him, and that"
"1304"	"he would be glad to see his long-forgotten old nurse and treat her"
"1305"	"gently. That would be lovely; that would make her forget her woes and"
"1306"	"her poverty."
"1307"	"Her poverty! That thought inspired her to add another castle to her"
"1308"	"dream: maybe he would give her a trifle now and then--maybe a dollar,"
"1309"	"once a month, say; any little thing like that would help, oh, ever so"
"1310"	"much."
"1311"	"By the time she reached Dawson's Landing she was her old self again; her"
"1312"	"blues were gone, she was in high feather. She would get along, surely;"
"1313"	"there were many kitchens where the servants would share their meals with"
"1314"	"her, and also steal sugar and apples and other dainties for her to carry"
"1315"	"home--or give her a chance to pilfer them herself, which would answer"
"1316"	"just as well. And there was the church. She was a more rabid and devoted"
"1317"	"Methodist than ever, and her piety was no sham, but was strong and"
"1318"	"sincere. Yes, with plenty of creature comforts and her old place in the"
"1319"	"amen-corner in her possession again, she would be perfectly happy and at"
"1320"	"peace thenceforward to the end."
"1321"	"She went to Judge Driscoll's kitchen first of all. She was received"
"1322"	"there in great form and with vast enthusiasm. Her wonderful travels, and"
"1323"	"the strange countries she had seen and the adventures she had had, made"
"1324"	"her a marvel, and a heroine of romance. The negroes hung enchanted upon"
"1325"	"the great story of her experiences, interrupting her all along with"
"1326"	"eager questions, with laughter, exclamations of delight and expressions"
"1327"	"of applause; and she was obliged to confess to herself that if there was"
"1328"	"anything better in this world than steamboating, it was the glory to be"
"1329"	"got by telling about it. The audience loaded her stomach with their"
"1330"	"dinners, and then stole the pantry bare to load up her basket."
"1331"	"Tom was in St. Louis. The servants said he had spent the best part of"
"1332"	"his time there during the previous two years. Roxy came every day, and"
"1333"	"had many talks about the family and its affairs. Once she asked why Tom"
"1334"	"was away so much. The ostensible \"Chambers\" said:"
"1335"	"\"De fac' is, ole marster kin git along better when young marster's away"
"1336"	"den he kin when he's in de town; yes, en he love him better, too; so he"
"1337"	"gives him fifty dollahs a month--\""
"1338"	"\"No, is dat so? Chambers, you's a-jokin', ain't you?\""
"1339"	"\"'Clah to goodness I ain't, mammy; Marse Tom tole me so his own self."
"1340"	"But nemmine, 'tain't enough.\""
"1341"	"\"My lan', what de reason 'tain't enough?\""
"1342"	"\"Well, I's gwine to tell you, if you gimme a chanst, mammy. De reason it"
"1343"	"ain't enough is 'ca'se Marse Tom gambles.\""
"1344"	"Roxy threw up her hands in astonishment and Chambers went on--"
"1345"	"\"Ole marster found it out, 'ca'se he had to pay two hundred dollahs for"
"1346"	"Marse Tom's gamblin' debts, en dat's true, mammy, jes as dead certain as"
"1347"	"you's bawn.\""
"1348"	"\"Two--hund'd--dollahs! Why, what is you talkin' 'bout? "
"1349"	"Two--hund'd--dollahs. Sakes alive, it's 'mos' enough to buy a tol'able"
"1350"	"good second-hand nigger wid. En you ain't lyin', honey?--you wouldn't"
"1351"	"lie to yo' ole mammy?\""
"1352"	"\"It's God's own truth, jes as I tell you--two hund'd dollahs--I wisht I"
"1353"	"may never stir outen my tracks if it ain't so. En, oh, my lan', ole"
"1354"	"Marse was jes a-hoppin'! he was b'ilin' mad, I tell you! He tuck 'n'"
"1355"	"dissenhurrit him.\""
"1356"	"He licked his chops with relish after that stately word. Roxy struggled"
"1357"	"with it a moment, then gave it up and said--"
"1358"	"\"Dissenwhiched him?\""
"1359"	"\"Dissenhurrit him.\""
"1360"	"\"What's dat? What do it mean?\""
"1361"	"\"Means he bu'sted de will.\""
"1362"	"\"Bu's--ted de will! He wouldn't ever treat him so! Take it back, you"
"1363"	"mis'able imitation nigger dat I bore in sorrow en tribbilation.\""
"1364"	"Roxy's pet castle--an occasional dollar from Tom's pocket--was tumbling"
"1365"	"to ruin before her eyes. She could not abide such a disaster as that;"
"1366"	"she couldn't endure the thought of it. Her remark amused Chambers:"
"1367"	"\"Yah-yah-yah! jes listen to dat! If I's imitation, what is you? Bofe of"
"1368"	"us is imitation white--dat's what we is--en pow'ful good imitation,"
"1369"	"too--yah-yah-yah!--we don't 'mount to noth'n as imitation niggers; en as"
"1370"	"for--\""
"1371"	"\"Shet up yo' foolin', 'fo' I knock you side de head, en tell me 'bout de"
"1372"	"will. Tell me 'tain't bu'sted--do, honey, en I'll never forgit you.\""
"1373"	"\"Well, 'tain't--'ca'se dey's a new one made, en Marse Tom's all right"
"1374"	"ag'in. But what is you in sich a sweat 'bout it for, mammy? 'Tain't none"
"1375"	"o' your business I don't reckon.\""
"1376"	"\"'Tain't none o' my business? Whose business is it den, I'd like to"
"1377"	"know? Wuz I his mother tell he was fifteen years old, or wusn't I?--you"
"1378"	"answer me dat. En you speck I could see him turned out po' en ornery on"
"1379"	"de worl' en never care noth'n' 'bout it? I reckon if you'd ever be'n a"
"1380"	"mother yo'self, Valet de Chambers, you wouldn't talk sich foolishness as"
"1381"	"dat.\""
"1382"	"\"Well, den, ole Marse forgive him en fixed up de will ag'in--do dat"
"1383"	"satisfy you?\""
"1384"	"Yes, she was satisfied now, and quite happy and sentimental over it. She"
"1385"	"kept coming daily, and at last she was told that Tom had come home. She"
"1386"	"began to tremble with emotion, and straightway sent to beg him to let"
"1387"	"his \"po' ole nigger mammy have jes one sight of him en die for joy.\""
"1388"	"Tom was stretched at his lazy ease on a sofa when Chambers brought the"
"1389"	"petition. Time had not modified his ancient detestation of the humble"
"1390"	"drudge and protector of his boyhood; it was still bitter and"
"1391"	"uncompromising. He sat up and bent a severe gaze upon the fair face of"
"1392"	"the young fellow whose name he was unconsciously using and whose family"
"1393"	"rights he was enjoying. He maintained the gaze until the victim of it"
"1394"	"had become satisfactorily pallid with terror, then he said--"
"1395"	"\"What does the old rip want with me?\""
"1396"	"The petition was meekly repeated."
"1397"	"\"Who gave you permission to come and disturb me with the social"
"1398"	"attentions of niggers?\""
"1399"	"Tom had risen. The other young man was trembling now, visibly. He saw"
"1400"	"what was coming, and bent his head sideways, and put up his left arm to"
"1401"	"shield it. Tom rained cuffs upon the head and its shield, saying no"
"1402"	"word: the victim received each blow with a beseeching, \"Please, Marse"
"1403"	"Tom!--oh, please, Marse Tom!\" Seven blows--then Tom said, \"Face the"
"1404"	"door--march!\" He followed behind with one, two, three solid kicks. The"
"1405"	"last one helped the pure-white slave over the door-sill, and he limped"
"1406"	"away mopping his eyes with his old ragged sleeve. Tom shouted after him,"
"1407"	"\"Send her in!\""
"1408"	"Then he flung himself panting on the sofa again, and rasped out the"
"1409"	"remark, \"He arrived just at the right moment; I was full to the brim"
"1410"	"with bitter thinkings, and nobody to take it out of. How refreshing it"
"1411"	"was! I feel better.\""
"1412"	"Tom's mother entered now, closing the door behind her, and approached"
"1413"	"her son with all the wheedling and supplicating servilities that fear"
"1414"	"and interest can impart to the words and attitudes of the born slave."
"1415"	"She stopped a yard from her boy and made two or three admiring"
"1416"	"exclamations over his manly stature and general handsomeness, and Tom"
"1417"	"put an arm under his head and hoisted a leg over the sofa-back in order"
"1418"	"to look properly indifferent."
"1419"	"\"My lan', how you is growed, honey! 'Clah to goodness, I wouldn't"
"1420"	"a-knowed you, Marse Tom! 'deed I wouldn't! Look at me good; does you"
"1421"	"'member old Roxy?--does you know yo' old nigger mammy, honey? Well, now,"
"1422"	"I kin lay down en die in peace, 'ca'se I'se seed--\""
"1423"	"\"Cut it short, ------ it, cut it short! What is it you want?\""
"1424"	"\"You heah dat? Jes the same old Marse Tom, al'ays so gay and funnin' wid"
"1425"	"de ole mammy. I 'uz jes as shore--\""
"1426"	"\"Cut it short, I tell you, and get along! What do you want?\""
"1427"	"This was a bitter disappointment. Roxy had for so many days nourished"
"1428"	"and fondled and petted her notion that Tom would be glad to see his old"
"1429"	"nurse, and would make her proud and happy to the marrow with a cordial"
"1430"	"word or two, that it took two rebuffs to convince her that he was not"
"1431"	"funning, and that her beautiful dream was a fond and foolish vanity, a"
"1432"	"shabby and pitiful mistake. She was hurt to the heart, and so ashamed"
"1433"	"that for a moment she did not quite know what to do or how to act. Then"
"1434"	"her breast began to heave, the tears came, and in her forlornness she"
"1435"	"was moved to try that other dream of hers--an appeal to her boy's"
"1436"	"charity; and so, upon the impulse, and without reflection, she offered"
"1437"	"her supplication:"
"1438"	"\"Oh, Marse Tom, de po' ole mammy is in sich hard luck dese days; en"
"1439"	"she's kinder crippled in de arms and can't work, en if you could gimme a"
"1440"	"dollah--on'y jes one little dol--\""
"1441"	"Tom was on his feet so suddenly that the supplicant was startled into a"
"1442"	"jump herself."
"1443"	"\"A dollar!--give you a dollar! I've a notion to strangle you! Is that"
"1444"	"your errand here? Clear out! and be quick about it!\""
"1445"	"Roxy backed slowly toward the door. When she was half-way she stopped,"
"1446"	"and said mournfully:"
"1447"	"\"Marse Tom, I nussed you when you was a little baby, en I raised you all"
"1448"	"by myself tell you was 'most a young man; en now you is young en rich,"
"1449"	"en I is po' en gitt'n ole, en I come heah b'lievin' dat you would he'p"
"1450"	"de ole mammy 'long down de little road dat's lef' 'twix' her en de"
"1451"	"grave, en--\""
"1452"	"Tom relished this tune less than any that had preceded it, for it began"
"1453"	"to wake up a sort of echo in his conscience; so he interrupted and said"
"1454"	"with decision, though without asperity, that he was not in a situation"
"1455"	"to help her, and wasn't going to do it."
"1456"	"\"Ain't you ever gwine to he'p me, Marse Tom?\""
"1457"	"\"No! Now go away and don't bother me any more.\""
"1458"	"Roxy's head was down, in an attitude of humility. But now the fires of"
"1459"	"her old wrongs flamed up in her breast and began to burn fiercely. She"
"1460"	"raised her head slowly, till it was well up, and at the same time her"
"1461"	"great frame unconsciously assumed an erect and masterful attitude, with"
"1462"	"all the majesty and grace of her vanished youth in it. She raised her"
"1463"	"finger and punctuated with it:"
"1464"	"\"You has said de word. You has had yo' chance, en you has trompled it"
"1465"	"under yo' foot. When you git another one, you'll git down on yo' knees"
"1466"	"en beg for it!\""
"1467"	"A cold chill went to Tom's heart, he didn't know why; for he did not"
"1468"	"reflect that such words, from such an incongruous source, and so"
"1469"	"solemnly delivered, could not easily fail of that effect. However, he"
"1470"	"did the natural thing: he replied with bluster and mockery:"
"1471"	"\"You'll give me a chance--you! Perhaps I'd better get down on my knees"
"1472"	"now! But in case I don't--just for argument's sake--what's going to"
"1473"	"happen, pray?\""
"1474"	"\"Dis is what is gwine to happen. I's gwine as straight to yo' uncle as I"
"1475"	"kin walk, en tell him every las' thing I knows 'bout you.\""
"1476"	"Tom's cheek blenched, and she saw it. Disturbing thoughts began to chase"
"1477"	"each other through his head. \"How can she know? And yet she must have"
"1478"	"found out--she looks it. I've had the will back only three months, and"
"1479"	"am already deep in debt again, and moving heaven and earth to save"
"1480"	"myself from exposure and destruction, with a reasonably fair show of"
"1481"	"getting the thing covered up if I'm let alone, and now this fiend has"
"1482"	"gone and found me out somehow or other. I wonder how much she knows? Oh,"
"1483"	"oh, oh, it's enough to break a body's heart! But I've got to humor"
"1484"	"her--there's no other way.\""
"1485"	"Then he worked up a rather sickly sample of a gay laugh and a hollow"
"1486"	"chipperness of manner, and said:"
"1487"	"\"Well, well, Roxy dear, old friends like you and me mustn't quarrel."
"1488"	"Here's your dollar--now tell me what you know.\""
"1489"	"He held out the wild-cat bill; she stood as she was, and made no"
"1490"	"movement. It was her turn to scorn persuasive foolery, now, and she did"
"1491"	"not waste it. She said, with a grim implacability in voice and manner"
"1492"	"which made Tom almost realize that even a former slave can remember for"
"1493"	"ten minutes insults and injuries returned for compliments and flatteries"
"1494"	"received, and can also enjoy taking revenge for them when the"
"1495"	"opportunity offers:"
"1496"	"\"What does I know? I'll tell you what I knows. I knows enough to bu'st"
"1497"	"dat will to flinders--en more, mind you, more!\""
"1498"	"Tom was aghast."
"1499"	"\"More?\" he said. \"What do you call more? Where's there any room for"
"1500"	"more?\""
"1501"	"Roxy laughed a mocking laugh, and said scoffingly, with a toss of her"
"1502"	"head, and her hands on her hips--"
"1503"	"\"Yes!--oh, I reckon! Co'se you'd like to know--wid yo' po' little ole"
"1504"	"rag dollah. What you reckon I's gwine to tell you for?--you ain't got no"
"1505"	"money. I's gwine to tell yo' uncle--en I'll do it dis minute, too--he'll"
"1506"	"gimme five dollahs for de news, en mighty glad, too.\""
"1507"	"She swung herself around disdainfully, and started away. Tom was in a"
"1508"	"panic. He seized her skirts, and implored her to wait. She turned and"
"1509"	"said, loftily--"
"1510"	"\"Look-a-heah, what 'uz it I tole you?\""
"1511"	"\"You--you--I don't remember anything. What was it you told me?\""
"1512"	"\"I tole you dat de next time I give you a chance you'd git down on yo'"
"1513"	"knees en beg for it.\""
"1514"	"Tom was stupefied for a moment. He was panting with excitement. Then he"
"1515"	"said:"
"1516"	"\"Oh, Roxy, you wouldn't require your young master to do such a horrible"
"1517"	"thing. You can't mean it.\""
"1518"	"\"I'll let you know mighty quick whether I means it or not! You call me"
"1519"	"names, en as good as spit on me when I comes here po' en ornery en"
"1520"	"'umble, to praise you for bein' growed up so fine en handsome, en tell"
"1521"	"you how I used to nuss you en tend you en watch you when you 'uz sick en"
"1522"	"hadn't no mother but me in de whole worl', en beg you to give de po' ole"
"1523"	"nigger a dollah for to git her som'n' to eat, en you call me"
"1524"	"names--names, dad blame you! Yassir, I gives you jes one chance mo', and"
"1525"	"dat's now, en it las' on'y a half a second--you hear?\""
"1526"	"Tom slumped to his knees and began to beg, saying--"
"1527"	"\"You see, I'm begging, and it's honest begging, too! Now tell me, Roxy,"
"1528"	"tell me.\""
"1529"	"The heir of two centuries of unatoned insult and outrage looked down on"
"1530"	"him and seemed to drink in deep draughts of satisfaction. Then she"
"1531"	"said--"
"1532"	"\"Fine nice young white gen'l'man kneelin' down to a nigger-wench! I's"
"1533"	"wanted to see dat jes once befo' I's called. Now, Gabr'el, blow de hawn,"
"1534"	"I's ready ... Git up!\""
"1535"	"Tom did it. He said, humbly--"
"1536"	"\"Now, Roxy, don't punish me any more. I deserved what I've got, but be"
"1537"	"good and let me off with that. Don't go to uncle. Tell me--I'll give you"
"1538"	"the five dollars.\""
"1539"	"\"Yes, I bet you will; en you won't stop dah, nuther. But I ain't gwine"
"1540"	"to tell you heah--\""
"1541"	"\"Good gracious, no!\""
"1542"	"\"Is you 'feared o' de ha'nted house?\""
"1543"	"\"N-no.\""
"1544"	"\"Well, den, you come to de ha'nted house 'bout ten or 'leven to-night,"
"1545"	"en climb up de ladder, 'ca'se de sta'r-steps is broke down, en you'll"
"1546"	"find me. I's a-roostin' in de ha'nted house 'ca'se I can't 'ford to"
"1547"	"roos' nowhers' else.\" She started toward the door, but stopped and said,"
"1548"	"\"Gimme de dollah bill!\" He gave it to her. She examined it and said,"
"1549"	"\"H'm--like enough de bank's bu'sted.\" She started again, but halted"
"1550"	"again. \"Has you got any whisky?\""
"1551"	"\"Yes, a little.\""
"1552"	"\"Fetch it!\""
"1553"	"He ran to his room overhead and brought down a bottle which was"
"1554"	"two-thirds full. She tilted it up and took a drink. Her eyes sparkled"
"1555"	"with satisfaction, and she tucked the bottle under her shawl, saying,"
"1556"	"\"It's prime. I'll take it along.\""
"1557"	"Tom humbly held the door for her, and she marched out as grim and erect"
"1558"	"as a grenadier."
"1559"	"CHAPTER IX."
"1560"	"Tom Practises Sycophancy."
"1561"	"Why is it that we rejoice at a birth and grieve at a funeral? It is"
"1562"	"because we are not the person involved.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"1563"	"It is easy to find fault, if one has that disposition. There was once a"
"1564"	"man who, not being able to find any other fault with his coal,"
"1565"	"complained that there were too many prehistoric toads in it.--Pudd'nhead"
"1566"	"Wilson's Calendar."
"1567"	"Tom flung himself on the sofa, and put his throbbing head in his hands,"
"1568"	"and rested his elbows on his knees. He rocked himself back and forth and"
"1569"	"moaned."
"1570"	"\"I've knelt to a nigger wench!\" he muttered. \"I thought I had struck the"
"1571"	"deepest depths of degradation before, but oh, dear, it was nothing to"
"1572"	"this.... Well, there is one consolation, such as it is--I've struck"
"1573"	"bottom this time; there's nothing lower.\""
"1574"	"But that was a hasty conclusion."
"1575"	"At ten that night he climbed the ladder in the haunted house, pale, weak"
"1576"	"and wretched. Roxy was standing in the door of one of the rooms,"
"1577"	"waiting, for she had heard him."
"1578"	"This was a two-story log house which had acquired the reputation a few"
"1579"	"years before of being haunted, and that was the end of its usefulness."
"1580"	"Nobody would live in it afterward, or go near it by night, and most"
"1581"	"people even gave it a wide berth in the daytime. As it had no"
"1582"	"competition, it was called the haunted house. It was getting crazy and"
"1583"	"ruinous, now, from long neglect. It stood three hundred yards beyond"
"1584"	"Pudd'nhead Wilson's house, with nothing between but vacancy. It was the"
"1585"	"last house in the town at that end."
"1586"	"Tom followed Roxy into the room. She had a pile of clean straw in the"
"1587"	"corner for a bed, some cheap but well-kept clothing was hanging on the"
"1588"	"wall, there was a tin lantern freckling the floor with little spots of"
"1589"	"light, and there were various soap-and-candle boxes scattered about,"
"1590"	"which served for chairs. The two sat down. Roxy said--"
"1591"	"\"Now den, I'll tell you straight off, en I'll begin to k'leck de money"
"1592"	"later on; I ain't in no hurry. What does you reckon I's gwine to tell"
"1593"	"you?\""
"1594"	"\"Well, you--you--oh, Roxy, don't make it too hard for me! Come right out"
"1595"	"and tell me you've found out somehow what a shape I'm in on account of"
"1596"	"dissipation and foolishness.\""
"1597"	"\"Disposition en foolishness! No sir, dat ain't it. Dat jist ain't"
"1598"	"nothin' at all, 'longside o' what I knows.\""
"1599"	"Tom stared at her, and said--"
"1600"	"\"Why, Roxy, what do you mean?\""
"1601"	"She rose, and gloomed above him like a Fate."
"1602"	"\"I means dis--en it's de Lord's truth. You ain't no more kin to ole"
"1603"	"Marse Driscoll den I is!--dat's what I means!\" and her eyes flamed with"
"1604"	"triumph."
"1605"	"\"What!\""
"1606"	"\"Yassir, en dat ain't all! You's a nigger!--bawn a nigger en a"
"1607"	"slave!--en you's a nigger en a slave dis minute; en if I opens my mouf"
"1608"	"ole Marse Driscoll'll sell you down de river befo' you is two days older"
"1609"	"den what you is now!\""
"1610"	"\"It's a thundering lie, you miserable old blatherskite!\""
"1611"	"\"It ain't no lie, nuther. It's jes de truth, en nothin' but de truth, so"
"1612"	"he'p me. Yassir--you's my son--\""
"1613"	"\"You devil!\""
"1614"	"\"En dat po' boy dat you's be'n a-kickin' en a-cuffin' to-day is Percy"
"1615"	"Driscoll's son en yo' marster--\""
"1616"	"\"You beast!\""
"1617"	"\"En his name's Tom Driscoll, en yo' name's Valet de Chambers, en you"
"1618"	"ain't got no fambly name, beca'se niggers don't have em!\""
"1619"	"Tom sprang up and seized a billet of wood and raised raised it; but his"
"1620"	"mother only laughed at him, and said--"
"1621"	"\"Set down, you pup! Does you think you kin skyer me? It ain't in you,"
"1622"	"nor de likes of you. I reckon you'd shoot me in de back, maybe, if you"
"1623"	"got a chance, for dat's jist yo' style--I knows you, throo en throo--but"
"1624"	"I don't mind gitt'n killed, beca'se all dis is down in writin' en it's"
"1625"	"in safe hands, too, en de man dat's got it knows whah to look for de"
"1626"	"right man when I gits killed. Oh, bless yo' soul, if you puts yo' mother"
"1627"	"up for as big a fool as you is, you's pow'ful mistaken, I kin tell you!"
"1628"	"Now den, you set still en behave yo'self; en don't you git up ag'in till"
"1629"	"I tell you!\""
"1630"	"Tom fretted and chafed awhile in a whirlwind of disorganizing sensations"
"1631"	"and emotions, and finally said, with something like settled conviction--"
"1632"	"\"The whole thing is moonshine; now then, go ahead and do your worst; I'm"
"1633"	"done with you.\""
"1634"	"Roxy made no answer. She took the lantern and started toward the door."
"1635"	"Tom was in a cold panic in a moment."
"1636"	"\"Come back, come back!\" he wailed. \"I didn't mean it, Roxy; I take it"
"1637"	"all back, and I'll never say it again! Please come back, Roxy!\""
"1638"	"The woman stood a moment, then she said gravely:"
"1639"	"\"Dat's one thing you's got to stop, Valet de Chambers. You can't call me"
"1640"	"Roxy, same as if you was my equal. Chillen don't speak to dey mammies"
"1641"	"like dat. You'll call me ma or mammy, dat's what you'll call"
"1642"	"me--leastways when dey ain't nobody aroun'. Say it!\""
"1643"	"It cost Tom a struggle, but he got it out."
"1644"	"\"Dat's all right. Don't you ever forgit it ag'in, if you knows what's"
"1645"	"good for you. Now den, you has said you wouldn't ever call it lies en"
"1646"	"moonshine ag'in. I'll tell you dis, for a warnin': if you ever does say"
"1647"	"it ag'in, it's de las' time you'll ever say it to me; I'll tramp as"
"1648"	"straight to de Judge as I kin walk, en tell him who you is, en prove it."
"1649"	"Does you b'lieve me when I says dat?\""
"1650"	"\"Oh,\" groaned Tom, \"I more than believe it; I know it.\""
"1651"	"Roxy knew her conquest was complete. She could have proved nothing to"
"1652"	"anybody, and her threat about the writings was a lie; but she knew the"
"1653"	"person she was dealing with, and had made both statements without any"
"1654"	"doubt as to the effect they would produce."
"1655"	"She went and sat down on her candle-box, and the pride and pomp of her"
"1656"	"victorious attitude made it a throne. She said--"
"1657"	"\"Now den, Chambers, we's gwine to talk business, en dey ain't gwine to"
"1658"	"be no mo' foolishness. In de fust place, you gits fifty dollahs a month;"
"1659"	"you's gwine to han' over half of it to yo' ma. Plank it out!\""
"1660"	"But Tom had only six dollars in the world. He gave her that, and"
"1661"	"promised to start fair on next month's pension."
"1662"	"\"Chambers, how much is you in debt?\""
"1663"	"Tom shuddered, and said--"
"1664"	"\"Nearly three hundred dollars.\""
"1665"	"\"How is you gwine to pay it?\""
"1666"	"Tom groaned out--\"Oh, I don't know; don't ask me such awful questions.\""
"1667"	"But she stuck to her point until she wearied a confession out of him: he"
"1668"	"had been prowling about in disguise, stealing small valuables from"
"1669"	"private houses; in fact, he made a good deal of a raid on his"
"1670"	"fellow-villagers a fortnight before, when he was supposed to be in St."
"1671"	"Louis; but he doubted if he had sent away enough stuff to realize the"
"1672"	"required amount, and was afraid to make a further venture in the present"
"1673"	"excited state of the town. His mother approved of his conduct, and"
"1674"	"offered to help, but this frightened him. He tremblingly ventured to say"
"1675"	"that if she would retire from the town he should feel better and safer,"
"1676"	"and could hold his head higher--and was going on to make an argument,"
"1677"	"but she interrupted and surprised him pleasantly by saying she was"
"1678"	"ready; it didn't make any difference to her where she stayed, so that"
"1679"	"she got her share of the pension regularly. She said she would not go"
"1680"	"far, and would call at the haunted house once a month for her money."
"1681"	"Then she said--"
"1682"	"\"I don't hate you so much now, but I've hated you a many a year--and"
"1683"	"anybody would. Didn't I change you off, en give you a good fambly en a"
"1684"	"good name, en made you a white gen'l'man en rich, wid store clothes"
"1685"	"on--en what did I git for it? You despised me all de time, en was al'ays"
"1686"	"sayin' mean hard things to me befo' folks, en wouldn't ever let me"
"1687"	"forgit I's a nigger--en--en------\""
"1688"	"She fell to sobbing, and broke down. Tom said--\"But you know I didn't"
"1689"	"know you were my mother; and besides--\""
"1690"	"\"Well, nemmine 'bout dat, now; let it go. I's gwine to fo'git it.\" Then"
"1691"	"she added fiercely, \"En don't ever make me remember it ag'in, or you'll"
"1692"	"be sorry, I tell you.\""
"1693"	"When they were parting, Tom said, in the most persuasive way he could"
"1694"	"command--"
"1695"	"\"Ma, would you mind telling me who was my father?\""
"1696"	"He had supposed he was asking an embarrassing question. He was mistaken."
"1697"	"Roxy drew herself up with a proud toss of her head, and said--"
"1698"	"\"Does I mine tellin' you? No, dat I don't! You ain't got no 'casion to"
"1699"	"be shame' o' yo' father, I kin tell you. He wuz de highest quality in"
"1700"	"dis whole town--ole Virginny stock. Fust famblies, he wuz. Jes as good"
"1701"	"stock as de Driscolls en de Howards, de bes' day dey ever seed.\" She put"
"1702"	"on a little prouder air, if possible, and added impressively: \"Does you"
"1703"	"'member Cunnel Cecil Burleigh Essex, dat died de same year yo' young"
"1704"	"Marse Tom Driscoll's pappy died, en all de Masons en Odd Fellers en"
"1705"	"Churches turned out en give him de bigges' funeral dis town ever seed?"
"1706"	"Dat's de man.\""
"1707"	"Under the inspiration of her soaring complacency the departed graces of"
"1708"	"her earlier days returned to her, and her bearing took to itself a"
"1709"	"dignity and state that might have passed for queenly if her surroundings"
"1710"	"had been a little more in keeping with it."
"1711"	"\"Dey ain't another nigger in dis town dat's as high-bawn as you is. Now"
"1712"	"den, go 'long! En jes you hold yo' head up as high as you want to--you"
"1713"	"has de right, en dat I kin swah.\""
"1714"	"CHAPTER X."
"1715"	"The Nymph Revealed."
"1716"	"All say, \"How hard it is that we have to die\"--a strange complaint to"
"1717"	"come from the mouths of people who have had to live.--Pudd'nhead"
"1718"	"Wilson's Calendar."
"1719"	"When angry, count four; when very angry, swear.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's"
"1720"	"Calendar."
"1721"	"Every now and then, after Tom went to bed, he had sudden wakings out of"
"1722"	"his sleep, and his first thought was, \"Oh, joy, it was all a dream!\""
"1723"	"Then he laid himself heavily down again, with a groan and the muttered"
"1724"	"words, \"A nigger! I am a nigger! Oh, I wish I was dead!\""
"1725"	"He woke at dawn with one more repetition of this horror, and then he"
"1726"	"resolved to meddle no more with that treacherous sleep. He began to"
"1727"	"think. Sufficiently bitter thinkings they were. They wandered along"
"1728"	"something after this fashion:"
"1729"	"\"Why were niggers and whites made? What crime did the uncreated first"
"1730"	"nigger commit that the curse of birth was decreed for him? And why is"
"1731"	"this awful difference made between white and black? ... How hard the"
"1732"	"nigger's fate seems, this morning!--yet until last night such a thought"
"1733"	"never entered my head.\""
"1734"	"He sighed and groaned an hour or more away. Then \"Chambers\" came humbly"
"1735"	"in to say that breakfast was nearly ready. \"Tom\" blushed scarlet to see"
"1736"	"this aristocratic white youth cringe to him, a nigger, and call him"
"1737"	"\"Young Marster.\" He said roughly--"
"1738"	"\"Get out of my sight!\" and when the youth was gone, he muttered, \"He has"
"1739"	"done me no harm, poor wretch, but he is an eyesore to me now, for he is"
"1740"	"Driscoll the young gentleman, and I am a--oh, I wish I was dead!\""
"1741"	"A gigantic irruption, like that of Krakatoa a few years ago, with the"
"1742"	"accompanying earthquakes, tidal waves, and clouds of volcanic dust,"
"1743"	"changes the face of the surrounding landscape beyond recognition,"
"1744"	"bringing down the high lands, elevating the low, making fair lakes where"
"1745"	"deserts had been, and deserts where green prairies had smiled before."
"1746"	"The tremendous catastrophe which had befallen Tom had changed his moral"
"1747"	"landscape in much the same way. Some of his low places he found lifted"
"1748"	"to ideals, some of his ideals had sunk to the valleys, and lay there"
"1749"	"with the sackcloth and ashes of pumice-stone and sulphur on their ruined"
"1750"	"heads."
"1751"	"For days he wandered in lonely places, thinking, thinking,"
"1752"	"thinking--trying to get his bearings. It was new work. If he met a"
"1753"	"friend, he found that the habit of a lifetime had in some mysterious way"
"1754"	"vanished--his arm hung limp, instead of involuntarily extending the hand"
"1755"	"for a shake. It was the \"nigger\" in him asserting its humility, and he"
"1756"	"blushed and was abashed. And the \"nigger\" in him was surprised when the"
"1757"	"white friend put out his hand for a shake with him. He found the"
"1758"	"\"nigger\" in him involuntarily giving the road, on the sidewalk, to a"
"1759"	"white rowdy and loafer. When Rowena, the dearest thing his heart knew,"
"1760"	"the idol of his secret worship, invited him in, the \"nigger\" in him made"
"1761"	"an embarrassed excuse and was afraid to enter and sit with the dread"
"1762"	"white folks on equal terms. The \"nigger\" in him went shrinking and"
"1763"	"skulking here and there and yonder, and fancying it saw suspicion and"
"1764"	"maybe detection in all faces, tones, and gestures. So strange and"
"1765"	"uncharacteristic was Tom's conduct that people noticed it, and turned to"
"1766"	"look after him when he passed on; and when he glanced back--as he could"
"1767"	"not help doing, in spite of his best resistance--and caught that puzzled"
"1768"	"expression in a person's face, it gave him a sick feeling, and he took"
"1769"	"himself out of view as quickly as he could. He presently came to have a"
"1770"	"hunted sense and a hunted look, and then he fled away to the hill-tops"
"1771"	"and the solitudes. He said to himself that the curse of Ham was upon"
"1772"	"him."
"1773"	"He dreaded his meals; the \"nigger\" in him was ashamed to sit at the"
"1774"	"white folks' table, and feared discovery all the time; and once when"
"1775"	"Judge Driscoll said, \"What's the matter with you? You look as meek as a"
"1776"	"nigger,\" he felt as secret murderers are said to feel when the accuser"
"1777"	"says, \"Thou art the man!\" Tom said he was not well, and left the table."
"1778"	"His ostensible \"aunt's\" solicitudes and endearments were become a terror"
"1779"	"to him, and he avoided them."
"1780"	"And all the time, hatred of his ostensible \"uncle\" was steadily growing"
"1781"	"in his heart; for he said to himself, \"He is white; and I am his"
"1782"	"chattel, his property, his goods, and he can sell me, just as he could"
"1783"	"his dog.\""
"1784"	"For as much as a week after this, Tom imagined that his character had"
"1785"	"undergone a pretty radical change. But that was because he did not know"
"1786"	"himself."
"1787"	"In several ways his opinions were totally changed, and would never go"
"1788"	"back to what they were before, but the main structure of his character"
"1789"	"was not changed, and could not be changed. One or two very important"
"1790"	"features of it were altered, and in time effects would result from this,"
"1791"	"if opportunity offered--effects of a quite serious nature, too. Under"
"1792"	"the influence of a great mental and moral upheaval his character and"
"1793"	"habits had taken on the appearance of complete change, but after a while"
"1794"	"with the subsidence of the storm both began to settle toward their"
"1795"	"former places. He dropped gradually back into his old frivolous and"
"1796"	"easy-going ways and conditions of feeling and manner of speech, and no"
"1797"	"familiar of his could have detected anything in him that differentiated"
"1798"	"him from the weak and careless Tom of other days."
"1799"	"The theft-raid which he had made upon the village turned out better than"
"1800"	"he had ventured to hope. It produced the sum necessary to pay his"
"1801"	"gaming-debts, and saved him from exposure to his uncle and another"
"1802"	"smashing of the will. He and his mother learned to like each other"
"1803"	"fairly well. She couldn't love him, as yet, because there \"warn't"
"1804"	"nothing to him,\" as she expressed it, but her nature needed something or"
"1805"	"somebody to rule over, and he was better than nothing. Her strong"
"1806"	"character and aggressive and commanding ways compelled Tom's admiration"
"1807"	"in spite of the fact that he got more illustrations of them than he"
"1808"	"needed for his comfort. However, as a rule her conversation was made up"
"1809"	"of racy tattle about the privacies of the chief families of the town"
"1810"	"(for she went harvesting among their kitchens every time she came to the"
"1811"	"village), and Tom enjoyed this. It was just in his line. She always"
"1812"	"collected her half of his pension punctually, and he was always at the"
"1813"	"haunted house to have a chat with her on these occasions. Every now and"
"1814"	"then she paid him a visit there on between-days also."
"1815"	"Occasionally he would run up to St. Louis for a few weeks, and at last"
"1816"	"temptation caught him again. He won a lot of money, but lost it, and"
"1817"	"with it a deal more besides, which he promised to raise as soon as"
"1818"	"possible."
"1819"	"For this purpose he projected a new raid on his town. He never meddled"
"1820"	"with any other town, for he was afraid to venture into houses whose ins"
"1821"	"and outs he did not know and the habits of whose households he was not"
"1822"	"acquainted with. He arrived at the haunted house in disguise on the"
"1823"	"Wednesday before the advent of the twins--after writing his aunt Pratt"
"1824"	"that he would not arrive until two days after--and lay in hiding there"
"1825"	"with his mother until toward daylight Friday morning, when he went to"
"1826"	"his uncle's house and entered by the back way with his own key, and"
"1827"	"slipped up to his room, where he could have the use of the mirror and"
"1828"	"toilet articles. He had a suit of girl's clothes with him in a bundle as"
"1829"	"a disguise for his raid, and was wearing a suit of his mother's"
"1830"	"clothing, with black gloves and veil. By dawn he was tricked out for his"
"1831"	"raid, but he caught a glimpse of Pudd'nhead Wilson through the window"
"1832"	"over the way, and knew that Pudd'nhead had caught a glimpse of him. So"
"1833"	"he entertained Wilson with some airs and graces and attitudes for a"
"1834"	"while, then stepped out of sight and resumed the other disguise, and by"
"1835"	"and by went down and out the back way and started down town to"
"1836"	"reconnoiter the scene of his intended labors."
"1837"	"But he was ill at ease. He had changed back to Roxy's dress, with the"
"1838"	"stoop of age added to the disguise, so that Wilson would not bother"
"1839"	"himself about a humble old woman leaving a neighbor's house by the back"
"1840"	"way in the early morning, in case he was still spying. But supposing"
"1841"	"Wilson had seen him leave, and had thought it suspicious, and had also"
"1842"	"followed him? The thought made Tom cold. He gave up the raid for the"
"1843"	"day, and hurried back to the haunted house by the obscurest route he"
"1844"	"knew. His mother was gone; but she came back, by and by, with the news"
"1845"	"of the grand reception at Patsy Cooper's, and soon persuaded him that"
"1846"	"the opportunity was like a special providence, it was so inviting and"
"1847"	"perfect. So he went raiding, after all, and made a nice success of it"
"1848"	"while everybody was gone to Patsy Cooper's. Success gave him nerve and"
"1849"	"even actual intrepidity; insomuch, indeed, that after he had conveyed"
"1850"	"his harvest to his mother in a back alley, he went to the reception"
"1851"	"himself, and added several of the valuables of that house to his"
"1852"	"takings."
"1853"	"After this long digression we have now arrived once more at the point"
"1854"	"where Pudd'nhead Wilson, while waiting for the arrival of the twins on"
"1855"	"that same Friday evening, sat puzzling over the strange apparition of"
"1856"	"that morning--a girl in young Tom Driscoll's bedroom; fretting, and"
"1857"	"guessing, and puzzling over it, and wondering who the shameless creature"
"1858"	"might be."
"1859"	"CHAPTER XI."
"1860"	"Pudd'nhead's Startling Discovery."
"1861"	"There are three infallible ways of pleasing an author, and the three"
"1862"	"form a rising scale of compliment: 1, to tell him you have read one of"
"1863"	"his books; 2, to tell him you have read all of his books; 3, to ask him"
"1864"	"to let you read the manuscript of his forthcoming book. No. 1 admits you"
"1865"	"to his respect; No. 2 admits you to his admiration; No. 3 carries you"
"1866"	"clear into his heart.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar."
"1867"	"As to the Adjective: when in doubt, strike it out.--Pudd'nhead Wilson's"
"1868"	"Calendar."
"1869"	"The twins arrived presently, and talk began. It flowed along chattily"
"1870"	"and sociably, and under its influence the new friendship gathered ease"
"1871"	"and strength. Wilson got out his Calendar, by request, and read a"
"1872"	"passage or two from it, which the twins praised quite cordially. This"
"1873"	"pleased the author so much that he complied gladly when they asked him"
"1874"	"to lend them a batch of the work to read at home. In the course of their"
"1875"	"wide travels they had found out that there are three sure ways of"
"1876"	"pleasing an author; they were now working the best of the three."
"1877"	"There was an interruption, now. Young Tom Driscoll appeared, and joined"
"1878"	"the party. He pretended to be seeing the distinguished strangers for the"
"1879"	"first time when they rose to shake hands; but this was only a blind, as"
"1880"	"he had already had a glimpse of them, at the reception, while robbing"
"1881"	"the house. The twins made mental note that he was smooth-faced and"
"1882"	"rather handsome, and smooth and undulatory in his movements--graceful,"
"1883"	"in fact. Angelo thought he had a good eye; Luigi thought there was"
"1884"	"something veiled and sly about it. Angelo thought he had a pleasant"
"1885"	"free-and-easy way of talking; Luigi thought it was more so than was"
"1886"	"agreeable. Angelo thought he was a sufficiently nice young man; Luigi"
"1887"	"reserved his decision. Tom's first contribution to the conversation was"
"1888"	"a question which he had put to Wilson a hundred times before. It was"
"1889"	"always cheerily and good-naturedly put, and always inflicted a little"
"1890"	"pang, for it touched a secret sore; but this time the pang was sharp,"
"1891"	"since strangers were present."
"1892"	"\"Well, how does the law come on? Had a case yet?\""
"1893"	"Wilson bit his lip, but answered, \"No--not yet,\" with as much"
"1894"	"indifference as he could assume. Judge Driscoll had generously left the"
"1895"	"law feature out of the Wilson biography which he had furnished to the"
"1896"	"twins. Young Tom laughed pleasantly, and said:"
"1897"	"\"Wilson's a lawyer, gentlemen, but he doesn't practise now.\""
"1898"	"The sarcasm bit, but Wilson kept himself under control, and said without"
"1899"	"passion:"
"1900"	"\"I don't practise, it is true. It is true that I have never had a case,"
"1901"	"and have had to earn a poor living for twenty years as an expert"
"1902"	"accountant in a town where I can't get hold of a set of books to"
"1903"	"untangle as often as I should like. But it is also true that I did fit"
"1904"	"myself well for the practice of the law. By the time I was your age,"
"1905"	"Tom, I had chosen a profession, and was soon competent to enter upon"
"1906"	"it.\" Tom winced. \"I never got a chance to try my hand at it, and I may"
"1907"	"never get a chance; and yet if I ever do get it I shall be found ready,"
"1908"	"for I have kept up my law-studies all these years.\""
"1909"	"\"That's it; that's good grit! I like to see it. I've a notion to throw"
"1910"	"all my business your way. My business and your law-practice ought to"
"1911"	"make a pretty gay team, Dave,\" and the young fellow laughed again."
"1912"	"\"If you will throw--\" Wilson had thought of the girl in Tom's bedroom,"
"1913"	"and was going to say, \"If you will throw the surreptitious and"
"1914"	"disreputable part of your business my way, it may amount to something;\""
"1915"	"but thought better of it and said, \"However, this matter doesn't fit"
"1916"	"well in a general conversation.\""
"1917"	"\"All right, we'll change the subject; I guess you were about to give me"
"1918"	"another dig, anyway, so I'm willing to change. How's the Awful Mystery"
"1919"	"flourishing these days? Wilson's got a scheme for driving plain"
"1920"	"window-glass out of the market by decorating it with greasy"
"1921"	"finger-marks, and getting rich by selling it at famine prices to the"
"1922"	"crowned heads over in Europe to outfit their palaces with. Fetch it out,"
"1923"	"Dave.\""
"1924"	"Wilson brought three of his glass strips, and said--"
"1925"	"\"I get the subject to pass the fingers of his right hand through his"
"1926"	"hair, so as to get a little coating of the natural oil on them, and then"
"1927"	"press the balls of them on the glass. A fine and delicate print of the"
"1928"	"lines in the skin results, and is permanent, if it doesn't come in"
"1929"	"contact with something able to rub it off. You begin, Tom.\""
"1930"	"\"Why, I think you took my finger-marks once or twice before.\""
"1931"	"\"Yes; but you were a little boy the last time, only about twelve years"
"1932"	"old.\""
"1933"	"\"That's so. Of course I've changed entirely since then, and variety is"
"1934"	"what the crowned heads want, I guess.\""
"1935"	"He passed his fingers through his crop of short hair, and pressed them"
"1936"	"one at a time on the glass. Angelo made a print of his fingers on"
"1937"	"another glass, and Luigi followed with the third. Wilson marked the"
"1938"	"glasses with names and date, and put them away. Tom gave one of his"
"1939"	"little laughs, and said--"
"1940"	"\"I thought I wouldn't say anything, but if variety is what you are"
"1941"	"after, you have wasted a piece of glass. The hand-print of one twin is"
"1942"	"the same as the hand-print of the fellow-twin.\""
"1943"	"\"Well, it's done now, and I like to have them both, anyway,\" said"
"1944"	"Wilson, returning to his place."
"1945"	"\"But look here, Dave,\" said Tom, \"you used to tell people's fortunes,"
"1946"	"too, when you took their finger-marks. Dave's just an all-round"
"1947"	"genius--a genius of the first water, gentlemen; a great scientist"
"1948"	"running to seed here in this village, a prophet with the kind of honor"
"1949"	"that prophets generally get at home--for here they don't give shucks for"
"1950"	"his scientifics, and they call his skull a notion-factory--hey, Dave,"
"1951"	"ain't it so? But never mind; he'll make his mark some day--finger-mark,"
"1952"	"you know, he-he! But really, you want to let him take a shy at your"
"1953"	"palms once; it's worth twice the price of admission or your money's"
"1954"	"returned at the door. Why, he'll read your wrinkles as easy as a book,"
"1955"	"and not only tell you fifty or sixty things that's going to happen to"
"1956"	"you, but fifty or sixty thousand that ain't. Come, Dave, show the"
"1957"	"gentlemen what an inspired Jack-at-all-science we've got in this town,"
"1958"	"and don't know it.\""
"1959"	"Wilson winced under this nagging and not very courteous chaff, and the"
"1960"	"twins suffered with him and for him. They rightly judged, now, that the"
"1961"	"best way to relieve him would be to take the thing in earnest and treat"
"1962"	"it with respect, ignoring Tom's rather overdone raillery; so Luigi"
"1963"	"said--"
"1964"	"\"We have seen something of palmistry in our wanderings, and know very"
"1965"	"well what astonishing things it can do. If it isn't a science, and one"
"1966"	"of the greatest of them, too, I don't know what its other name ought to"
"1967"	"be. In the Orient--\""
"1968"	"Tom looked surprised and incredulous. He said--"
"1969"	"\"That juggling a science? But really, you ain't serious, are you?\""
"1970"	"\"Yes, entirely so. Four years ago we had our hands read out to us as if"
"1971"	"our palms had been covered with print.\""
"1972"	"\"Well, do you mean to say there was actually anything in it?\" asked Tom,"
"1973"	"his incredulity beginning to weaken a little."
"1974"	"\"There was this much in it,\" said Angelo: \"what was told us of our"
"1975"	"characters was minutely exact--we could not have bettered it ourselves."
"1976"	"Next, two or three memorable things that had happened to us were laid"
"1977"	"bare--things which no one present but ourselves could have known about.\""
"1978"	"\"Why, it's rank sorcery!\" exclaimed Tom, who was now becoming very much"
"1979"	"interested. \"And how did they make out with what was going to happen to"
"1980"	"you in the future?\""
"1981"	"\"On the whole, quite fairly,\" said Luigi. \"Two or three of the most"
"1982"	"striking things foretold have happened since; much the most striking one"
"1983"	"of all happened within that same year. Some of the minor prophecies have"
"1984"	"come true; some of the minor and some of the major ones have not been"
"1985"	"fulfilled yet, and of course may never be: still, I should be more"
"1986"	"surprised if they failed to arrive than if they didn't.\""
"1987"	"Tom was entirely sobered, and profoundly impressed. He said,"
"1988"	"apologetically--"
"1989"	"\"Dave, I wasn't meaning to belittle that science; I was only"
"1990"	"chaffing--chattering, I reckon I'd better say. I wish you would look at"
"1991"	"their palms. Come, won't you?\""
"1992"	"\"Why certainly, if you want me to; but you know I've had no chance to"
"1993"	"become an expert, and don't claim to be one. When a past event is"
"1994"	"somewhat prominently recorded in the palm I can generally detect that,"
"1995"	"but minor ones often escape me,--not always, of course, but often,--but"
"1996"	"I haven't much confidence in myself when it comes to reading the future."
"1997"	"I am talking as if palmistry was a daily study with me, but that is not"
"1998"	"so. I haven't examined half a dozen hands in the last half dozen years;"
"1999"	"you see, the people got to joking about it, and I stopped to let the"
"2000"	"talk die down. I'll tell you what we'll do, Count Luigi: I'll make a try"
"2001"	"at your past, and if I have any success there--no, on the whole, I'll"
"2002"	"let the future alone; that's really the affair of an expert.\""
"2003"	"He took Luigi's hand. Tom said--"
"2004"	"\"Wait--don't look yet, Dave! Count Luigi, here's paper and pencil. Set"
"2005"	"down that thing that you said was the most striking one that was"
"2006"	"foretold to you, and happened less than a year afterward, and give it to"
"2007"	"me so I can see if Dave finds it in your hand.\""
"2008"	"Luigi wrote a line privately, and folded up the piece of paper, and"
"2009"	"handed it to Tom, saying--"
"2010"	"\"I'll tell you when to look at it, if he finds it.\""
"2011"	"Wilson began to study Luigi's palm, tracing life lines, heart lines,"
"2012"	"head lines, and so on, and noting carefully their relations with the"
"2013"	"cobweb of finer and more delicate marks and lines that enmeshed them on"
"2014"	"all sides; he felt of the fleshy cushion at the base of the thumb, and"
"2015"	"noted its shape; he felt of the fleshy side of the hand between the"
"2016"	"wrist and the base of the little finger, and noted its shape also; he"
"2017"	"painstakingly examined the fingers, observing their form, proportions,"
"2018"	"and natural manner of disposing themselves when in repose. All this"
"2019"	"process was watched by the three spectators with absorbing interest,"
"2020"	"their heads bent together over Luigi's palm, and nobody disturbing the"
"2021"	"stillness with a word. Wilson now entered upon a close survey of the"
"2022"	"palm again, and his revelations began."
"2023"	"He mapped out Luigi's character and disposition, his tastes, aversions,"
"2024"	"proclivities, ambitions, and eccentricities in a way which sometimes"
"2025"	"made Luigi wince and the others laugh, but both twins declared that the"
"2026"	"chart was artistically drawn and was correct."
"2027"	"Next, Wilson took up Luigi's history. He proceeded cautiously and with"
"2028"	"hesitation, now, moving his finger slowly along the great lines of the"
"2029"	"palm, and now and then halting it at a \"star\" or some such landmark, and"
"2030"	"examining that neighborhood minutely. He proclaimed one or two past"
"2031"	"events, Luigi confirmed his correctness, and the search went on."
"2032"	"Presently Wilson glanced up suddenly with a surprised expression--"
"2033"	"\"Here is a record of an incident which you would perhaps not wish me"
"2034"	"to--\""
"2035"	"\"Bring it out,\" said Luigi, good-naturedly; \"I promise you it sha'n't"
"2036"	"embarrass me.\""
"2037"	"But Wilson still hesitated, and did not seem quite to know what to do."
"2038"	"Then he said--"
"2039"	"\"I think it is too delicate a matter to--to--I believe I would rather"
"2040"	"write it or whisper it to you, and let you decide for yourself whether"
"2041"	"you want it talked out or not.\""
"2042"	"\"That will answer,\" said Luigi; \"write it.\""
"2043"	"Wilson wrote something on a slip of paper and handed it to Luigi, who"
"2044"	"read it to himself and said to Tom--"
"2045"	"\"Unfold your slip and read it, Mr. Driscoll.\""
"2046"	"Tom read:"
"2047"	"\"It was prophesied that I would kill a man. It came true before the year"
"2048"	"was out.\""
"2049"	"Tom added, \"Great Scott!\""
"2050"	"Luigi handed Wilson's paper to Tom, and said--"
"2051"	"\"Now read this one.\""
"2052"	"Tom read:"
"2053"	"\"You have killed some one, but whether man, woman or child, I do not"
"2054"	"make out.\""
"2055"	"\"Cæsar's ghost!\" commented Tom, with astonishment. \"It beats anything"
"2056"	"that was ever heard of! Why, a man's own hand is his deadliest enemy!"
"2057"	"Just think of that--a man's own hand keeps a record of the deepest and"
"2058"	"fatalest secrets of his life, and is treacherously ready to expose him"
"2059"	"to any black-magic stranger that comes along. But what do you let a"
"2060"	"person look at your hand for, with that awful thing printed on it?\""
"2061"	"\"Oh,\" said Luigi, reposefully, \"I don't mind it. I killed the man for"
"2062"	"good reasons, and I don't regret it.\""
"2063"	"\"What were the reasons?\""
"2064"	"\"Well, he needed killing.\""
"2065"	"\"I'll tell you why he did it, since he won't say himself,\" said Angelo,"
"2066"	"warmly. \"He did it to save my life, that's what he did it for. So it was"
"2067"	"a noble act, and not a thing to be hid in the dark.\""
"2068"	"\"So it was, so it was,\" said Wilson; \"to do such a thing to save a"
"2069"	"brother's life is a great and fine action.\""
"2070"	"\"Now come,\" said Luigi, \"it is very pleasant to hear you say these"
"2071"	"things, but for unselfishness, or heroism, or magnanimity, the"
"2072"	"circumstances won't stand scrutiny. You overlook one detail; suppose I"
"2073"	"hadn't saved Angelo's life, what would have become of mine? If I had let"
"2074"	"the man kill him, wouldn't he have killed me, too? I saved my own life,"
"2075"	"you see.\""
"2076"	"\"Yes, that is your way of talking,\" said Angelo, \"but I know you--I"
"2077"	"don't believe you thought of yourself at all. I keep that weapon yet"
"2078"	"that Luigi killed the man with, and I'll show it to you sometime. That"
"2079"	"incident makes it interesting, and it had a history before it came into"
"2080"	"Luigi's hands which adds to its interest. It was given to Luigi by a"
"2081"	"great Indian prince, the Gaikowar of Baroda, and it had been in his"
"2082"	"family two or three centuries. It killed a good many disagreeable people"
"2083"	"who troubled that hearthstone at one time and another. It isn't much too"
"2084"	"look at, except that it isn't shaped like other knives, or dirks, or"
"2085"	"whatever it may be called--here, I'll draw it for you.\" He took a sheet"
"2086"	"of paper and made a rapid sketch. \"There it is--a broad and murderous"
"2087"	"blade, with edges like a razor for sharpness. The devices engraved on it"
"2088"	"are the ciphers or names of its long line of possessors--I had Luigi's"
"2089"	"name added in Roman letters myself with our coat of arms, as you see."
"2090"	"You notice what a curious handle the thing has. It is solid ivory,"
"2091"	"polished like a mirror, and is four or five inches long--round, and as"
"2092"	"thick as a large man's wrist, with the end squared off flat, for your"
"2093"	"thumb to rest on; for you grasp it, with your thumb resting on the blunt"
"2094"	"end--so--and lift it aloft and strike downward. The Gaikowar showed us"
"2095"	"how the thing was done when he gave it to Luigi, and before that night"
"2096"	"was ended Luigi had used the knife, and the Gaikowar was a man short by"
"2097"	"reason of it. The sheath is magnificently ornamented with gems of great"
"2098"	"value. You will find the sheath more worth looking at than the knife"
"2099"	"itself, of course.\""
"2100"	"Tom said to himself--"
"2101"	"\"It's lucky I came here. I would have sold that knife for a song; I"
"2102"	"supposed the jewels were glass.\""
"2103"	"\"But go on; don't stop,\" said Wilson. \"Our curiosity is up now, to hear"
"2104"	"about the homicide. Tell us about that.\""
"2105"	"\"Well, briefly, the knife was to blame for that, all around. A native"
"2106"	"servant slipped into our room in the palace in the night, to kill us and"
"2107"	"steal the knife on account of the fortune incrusted on its sheath,"
"2108"	"without a doubt. Luigi had it under his pillow; we were in bed together."
"2109"	"There was a dim night-light burning. I was asleep, but Luigi was awake,"
"2110"	"and he thought he detected a vague form nearing the bed. He slipped the"
"2111"	"knife out of the sheath and was ready, and unembarrassed by hampering"
"2112"	"bed-clothes, for the weather was hot and we hadn't any. Suddenly that"
"2113"	"native rose at the bedside, and bent over me with his right hand lifted"
"2114"	"and a dirk in it aimed at my throat; but Luigi grabbed his wrist, pulled"
"2115"	"him downward, and drove his own knife into the man's neck. That is the"
"2116"	"whole story.\""
"2117"	"Wilson and Tom drew deep breaths, and after some general chat about the"
"2118"	"tragedy, Pudd'nhead said, taking Tom's hand--"
"2119"	"\"Now, Tom, I've never had a look at your palms, as it happens; perhaps"
"2120"	"you've got some little questionable privacies that need--hel-lo!\""
"2121"	"Tom had snatched away his hand, and was looking a good deal confused."
"2122"	"\"Why, he's blushing!\" said Luigi."
"2123"	"Tom darted an ugly look at him, and said sharply--"
"2124"	"\"Well, if I am, it ain't because I'm a murderer!\" Luigi's dark face"
"2125"	"flushed, but before he could speak or move, Tom added with anxious"
"2126"	"haste: \"Oh, I beg a thousand pardons. I didn't mean that; it was out"
"2127"	"before I thought, and I'm very, very sorry--you must forgive me!\""
"2128"	"Wilson came to the rescue, and smoothed things down as well as he could;"
"2129"	"and in fact was entirely successful as far as the twins were concerned,"
"2130"	"for they felt sorrier for the affront put upon him by his guest's"
"2131"	"outburst of ill manners than for the insult offered to Luigi. But the"
"2132"	"success was not so pronounced with the offender. Tom tried to seem at"
"2133"	"his ease, and he went through the motions fairly well, but at bottom he"
"2134"	"felt resentful toward all the three witnesses of his exhibition; in"
"2135"	"fact, he felt so annoyed at them for having witnessed it and noticed it"
"2136"	"that he almost forgot to feel annoyed at himself for placing it before"
"2137"	"them. However, something presently happened which made him almost"
"2138"	"comfortable, and brought him nearly back to a state of charity and"
"2139"	"friendliness. This was a little spat between the twins; not much of a"
"2140"	"spat, but still a spat; and before they got far with it they were in a"
"2141"	"decided condition of irritation with each other. Tom was charmed; so"
"2142"	"pleased, indeed, that he cautiously did what he could to increase the"
"2143"	"irritation while pretending to be actuated by more respectable motives."
"2144"	"By his help the fire got warmed up to the blazing-point, and he might"
"2145"	"have had the happiness of seeing the flames show up, in another moment,"
"2146"	"but for the interruption of a knock on the door--an interruption which"
"2147"	"fretted him as much as it gratified Wilson. Wilson opened the door."
"2148"	"The visitor was a good-natured, ignorant, energetic, middle-aged"
"2149"	"Irishman named John Buckstone, who was a great politician in a small"
"2150"	"way, and always took a large share in public matters of every sort. One"
"2151"	"of the town's chief excitements, just now, was over the matter of rum."
"2152"	"There was a strong rum party and a strong anti-rum party. Buckstone was"
"2153"	"training with the rum party, and he had been sent to hunt up the twins"
"2154"	"and invite them to attend a mass-meeting of that faction. He delivered"
"2155"	"his errand, and said the clans were already gathering in the big hall"
"2156"	"over the market-house. Luigi accepted the invitation cordially, Angelo"
"2157"	"less cordially, since he disliked crowds, and did not drink the powerful"
"2158"	"intoxicants of America. In fact, he was even a teetotaler"
"2159"	"sometimes--when it was judicious to be one."
"2160"	"The twins left with Buckstone, and Tom Driscoll joined company with them"
"2161"	"uninvited."
"2162"	"In the distance one could see a long wavering line of torches drifting"
"2163"	"down the main street, and could hear the throbbing of the bass drum, the"
"2164"	"clash of cymbals, the squeaking of a fife or two, and the faint roar of"
"2165"	"remote hurrahs. The tail-end of this procession was climbing the"
"2166"	"market-house stairs when the twins arrived in its neighborhood; when"
"2167"	"they reached the hall it was full of people, torches, smoke, noise and"
"2168"	"enthusiasm. They were conducted to the platform by Buckstone--Tom"
"2169"	"Driscoll still following--and were delivered to the chairman in the"
"2170"	"midst of a prodigious explosion of welcome. When the noise had moderated"
"2171"	"a little, the chair proposed that \"our illustrious guests be at once"
"2172"	"elected, by complimentary acclamation, to membership in our"
"2173"	"ever-glorious organization, the paradise of the free and the perdition"
"2174"	"of the slave.\""
"2175"	"This eloquent discharge opened the flood-gates of enthusiasm again, and"
"2176"	"the election was carried with thundering unanimity. Then arose a storm"
"2177"	"of cries:"
"2178"	"\"Wet them down! Wet them down! Give them a drink!\""
"2179"	"Glasses of whisky were handed to the twins. Luigi waved his aloft, then"
"2180"	"brought it to his lips; but Angelo set his down. There was another storm"
"2181"	"of cries:"
"2182"	"\"What's the matter with the other one?\" \"What is the blond one going"
"2183"	"back on us for?\" \"Explain! Explain!\""
"2184"	"The chairman inquired, and then reported--"
"2185"	"\"We have made an unfortunate mistake, gentlemen. I find that the Count"
"2186"	"Angelo Capello is opposed to our creed--is a teetotaler, in fact, and"
"2187"	"was not intending to apply for membership with us. He desires that we"
"2188"	"reconsider the vote by which he was elected. What is the pleasure of the"
"2189"	"house?\""
"2190"	"There was a general burst of laughter, plentifully accented with"
"2191"	"whistlings and cat-calls, but the energetic use of the gavel presently"
"2192"	"restored something like order. Then a man spoke from the crowd, and said"
"2193"	"that while he was very sorry that the mistake had been made, it would"
"2194"	"not be possible to rectify it at the present meeting. According to the"
"2195"	"by-laws it must go over to the next regular meeting for action. He would"
"2196"	"not offer a motion, as none was required. He desired to apologize to the"
"2197"	"gentleman in the name of the house, and begged to assure him that as far"
"2198"	"as it might lie in the power of the Sons of Liberty, his temporary"
"2199"	"membership in the order would be made pleasant to him."
"2200"	"This speech was received with great applause, mixed with cries of--"
"2201"	"\"That's the talk!\" \"He's a good fellow, anyway, if he is a teetotaler!\""
"2202"	"\"Drink his health!\" \"Give him a rouser, and no heeltaps!\""
"2203"	"Glasses were handed around, and everybody on the platform drank Angelo's"
"2204"	"health, while the house bellowed forth in song:"
"2205"	"   For he's a jolly good fel-low,"
"2206"	"   For he's a jolly good fel-low,"
"2207"	"   For he's a jolly good fe-el-low,--"
"2208"	"      Which nobody can deny."
"2209"	"Tom Driscoll drank. It was his second glass, for he had drunk Angelo's"
"2210"	"the moment that Angelo had set it down. The two drinks made him very"
"2211"	"merry--almost idiotically so--and he began to take a most lively and"
"2212"	"prominent part in the proceedings, particularly in the music and"
"2213"	"cat-calls and side-remarks."
"2214"	"The chairman was still standing at the front, the twins at his side. The"
"2215"	"extraordinarily close resemblance of the brothers to each other"
"2216"	"suggested a witticism to Tom Driscoll, and just as the chairman began a"
"2217"	"speech he skipped forward and said with an air of tipsy confidence to"
"2218"	"the audience--"
"2219"	"\"Boys, I move that he keeps still and lets this human philopena snip you"
"2220"	"out a speech.\""
"2221"	"The descriptive aptness of the phrase caught the house, and a mighty"
"2222"	"burst of laughter followed."
"2223"	"Luigi's southern blood leaped to the boiling-point in a moment under the"
"2224"	"sharp humiliation of this insult delivered in the presence of four"
"2225"	"hundred strangers. It was not in the young man's nature to let the"
"2226"	"matter pass, or to delay the squaring of the account. He took a couple"
"2227"	"of strides and halted behind the unsuspecting joker. Then he drew back"
"2228"	"and delivered a kick of such titanic vigor that it lifted Tom clear over"
"2229"	"the footlights and landed him on the heads of the front row of the Sons"
"2230"	"of Liberty."
"2231"	"Even a sober person does not like to have a human being emptied on him"
"2232"	"when he is not doing any harm; a person who is not sober cannot endure"
"2233"	"such an attention at all. The nest of Sons of Liberty that Driscoll"
"2234"	"landed in had not a sober bird in it; in fact there was probably not an"
"2235"	"entirely sober one in the auditorium. Driscoll was promptly and"
"2236"	"indignantly flung on to the heads of Sons in the next row, and these"
"2237"	"Sons passed him on toward the rear, and then immediately began to pummel"
"2238"	"the front-row Sons who had passed him to them. This course was strictly"
"2239"	"followed by bench after bench as Driscoll traveled in his tumultuous and"
"2240"	"airy flight toward the door; so he left behind him an ever lengthening"
"2241"	"wake of raging and plunging and fighting and swearing humanity. Down"
"2242"	"went group after group of torches, and presently above the deafening"
"2243"	"clatter of the gavel, roar of angry voices, and crash of succumbing"
"2244"	"benches, rose the paralyzing cry of \"Fire!\""
"2245"	"The fighting ceased instantly; the cursing ceased; for one distinctly"
"2246"	"defined moment there was a dead hush, a motionless calm, where the"
"2247"	"tempest had been; then with one impulse the multitude awoke to life and"
"2248"	"energy again, and went surging and struggling and swaying, this way and"
"2249"	"that, its outer edges melting away through windows and doors and"
"2250"	"gradually lessening the pressure and relieving the mass."
"2251"	"The fire-boys were never on hand so suddenly before; for there was no"
"2252"	"distance to go, this time, their quarters being in the rear end of the"
"2253"	"market-house. There was an engine company and a hook-and-ladder company."
"2254"	"Half of each was composed of rummies and the other half of anti-rummies,"
"2255"	"after the moral and political share-and-share-alike fashion of the"
"2256"	"frontier town of the period. Enough anti-rummies were loafing in"
"2257"	"quarters to man the engine and the ladders. In two minutes they had"
"2258"	"their red shirts and helmets on--they never stirred officially in"
"2259"	"unofficial costume--and as the mass meeting overhead smashed through the"
"2260"	"long row of windows and poured out upon the roof of the arcade, the"
"2261"	"deliverers were ready for them with a powerful stream of water which"
"2262"	"washed some of them off the roof and nearly drowned the rest. But water"
"2263"	"was preferable to fire, and still the stampede from the windows"
"2264"	"continued, and still the pitiless drenching assailed it until the"
"2265"	"building was empty; then the fire-boys mounted to the hall and flooded"
"2266"	"it with water enough to annihilate forty times as much fire as there was"
"2267"	"there; for a village fire-company does not often get a chance to show"
"2268"	"off, and so when it does get a chance it makes the most of it. Such"
"2269"	"citizens of that village as were of a thoughtful and judicious"
"2270"	"temperament did not insure against fire; they insured against the"
"2271"	"fire-company."
"2272"	"CHAPTER XII."
"2273"	"The Shame of Judge Driscoll."
"2274"	"Courage is resistance to fear, mastery of fear--not absence of fear."
"2275"	"Except a creature be part coward it is not a compliment to say it is"
"2276"	"brave; it is merely a loose misapplication of the word. Consider the"
"2277"	"flea!--incomparably the bravest of all the creatures of God, if"
"2278"	"ignorance of fear were courage. Whether you are asleep or awake he will"
"2279"	"attack you, caring nothing for the fact that in bulk and strength you"
"2280"	"are to him as are the massed armies of the earth to a sucking child; he"
"2281"	"lives both day and night and all days and nights in the very lap of"
"2282"	"peril and the immediate presence of death, and yet is no more afraid"
"2283"	"than is the man who walks the streets of a city that was threatened by"
"2284"	"an earthquake ten centuries before. When we speak of Clive, Nelson, and"
"2285"	"Putnam as men who \"didn't know what fear was,\" we ought always to add"
"2286"	"the flea--and put him at the head of the procession.--Pudd'nhead"
"2287"	"Wilson's Calendar."
"2288"	"Judge Driscoll was in bed and asleep by ten o'clock on Friday night, and"
"2289"	"he was up and gone a-fishing before daylight in the morning with his"
"2290"	"friend Pembroke Howard. These two had been boys together in Virginia"
"2291"	"when that State still ranked as the chief and most imposing member of"
"2292"	"the Union, and they still coupled the proud and affectionate adjective"
"2293"	"\"old\" with her name when they spoke of her. In Missouri a recognized"
